Preface
This project is a two times 35 ECTS points master thesis. It is written in corporation with the
Technical University of Denmark (DTU) and Burmeister & Wain Scandinavian Contractor (BWSC).
In the 2021 strategy plan from BWSC, focus on new markets is a key point. This focus on new
energy markets, has made it possible to write this master thesis in corporation with BWSC in order
to investigate a possible new market.
Throughout this master thesis, help and support has been provided by a number of people within
BWSC. A very special appreciation shall be given to our supervisor in BWSC Matias Pozzi. Matias
has in the whole period been very helpful with support, comments and questions to the written work.
Furthermore, we would like to thank BWSC in general. We have been allowed to ask and use a lot
of colleagues within BWSC for questions and data collection. BWSC has also invited us to two very
useful meetings with ABB, where some of the content in this project where enlightened.
Though this project is a combination of two master theses, a coherence between the diﬀerent parts
have been tried obtained. This is done in order to have a common thread through the project, which
make it seem like a single large project. The distribution of the diﬀerent chapters and sections is as
follows:
By Johan Pedersen
Chapter 2 up to section 2.8.1
Chapter 3
Chapter 4
Chapter 5
By Matthias Kaa Ingemann Johansen

From section 2.8.1 up to chapter 3

==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In this master thesis is it investigated how a BESS implementation in a hybrid power plant or a island
power grid, can contribute to stability and lower power production costs.
When the amount of renewable energy in the power grids increases, the stability of the grid can in
certain cases decreases. This due to uncontrollability of the renewable generation, less system inertia
among other stability parameters. Implementation of a BESS in an island power grid, can counteract
this due to diﬀerent abilities of the BESS. These abilities of the BESS is investigated and shown in
this project. Some of these are analysed with the use of a established test grid in PowerFactory, while
others are analysed with the use of an obtained PowerFactory ﬁle of the Mauritius power grid.
Simulation where the diﬀerent BESS abilities where anlysed showed that the BESS can contribute to
short circuit current contribution, perform frequency- and voltage support and capacitive ﬁrming.
Apart from contribute to keep grid stability, a BESS can also be used together with a power man-
agement system to decrease the total power production costs. This is done by decreasing the amount
of curtailed renewable power and increase the power production eﬃciency of the conventional power
plants.
In order to analyse how a BESS in coorporation with a power management system, can decrease the
total power production costs, two generation scheduling optimisation models have been established.
One for a hybrid power plant, and another for a power grid. With the use of the optimization model
for the hybrid power plant, the economical feasibility of a BESS implementation is calculated for a
hybrid power plant consisting of ﬁve diesel engines and a PV plant. By calculating the economical
results for a BESS implementation for diﬀerent sizes of PV and BESS, the best BESS and PV plant
size where found. In the used case, the best PV- and BESS size where 30 MW and 5 MWh respec-
tively. With this combination, the internal rate of return of the BESS implementation was calculated
to 0.97 %. Though in the simulations it was also shown that the price for BESS and fuel does not
have to change much before a BESS implementation is economically feasible.
With some extensions, the developed generation optimisation model for the hybrid power plant can
optimise the power generation for a entire power grid. With this extended model and data from
the Mauritius PowerFactory model, the economically best BESS placement where calculated. This
showed that the economically best BESS placement in the Mauritius grid where at the FUEL SE
busbar.

==PAGE:19==
1
Introduction
1.1
Project background
In the last decades, development of renewable energy sources has increased rapidly. Signiﬁcant de-
velopment of especially wind and solar energy has brought down the price of these to a point where
energy from wind and solar is price competitive with known conventional energy sources, such as coal
and diesel1.
Price competitiveness of renewable energy, support schemes and general ambitions to decrease emis-
sions in many countries, results in an increased amount of renewable energy in power grids around
the world. In larger systems, such as the European grid, a relatively large amount of renewable
power can be managed due to the grid size and interconnected countries. Though in smaller grids,
an implementation of renewable energy can cause grid instability and protection problems.
Implementation of renewable energy sources introduces challenges for the grid operator, due to the
ﬂuctuating power production and the possibilities for incorrect power production forecasts. This
results in increased requirements to the Power Management System (PMS) which needs to include
renewable generation in the power production. The target for such a system is to always control the
power production in such a way that the demand is covered at the lowest possible costs, while keeping
the grid stable at all times. With an increase of renewable energy, forecasts for wind and solar needs
to be taken into account, in order for the PMS to make an approximated prediction of the production
for the renewable energy sources.
The operation cost of the system is lowered when the amount of renewable energy in the system
increases, due to their nearly zero marginal production costs. This gives an incentive for the PMS to
maximize the amount of renewable energy in the system.
A solution to avoid curtailing of power from renewable energy sources and thereby lower power pro-
duction costs, is to implement Battery Energy Storage System (BESS) in the grid. Furthermore,
implementation of BESS can have several other positive eﬀects on a power system, such as lower
total emissions and improved stability of the power grid.
In 2013 the Central Electricity Board (CEB) made a new Integrated Electricity Plan (IEP) for
2013-2022 that outlined the plans for further development of the electricity grid in Mauritius. CEB
is the Mauritian transmission- and distribution system operator as well as power producer. The
previous IEP covered the period between 2003 and 2012, and since then Mauritius has experienced
an increase in demand. This has forced CEB to build new higher eﬃcient conventional power plants
in order to cover the increase in demand, ensure a stable grid operation and decommission some of
1http://borsen.dk/nyheder/virksomheder/artikel/1/343432/dong_vinder_tysk_vindmoelle-ordre_
paa_snedig_vis_vi_skal_ikke_have_en_eneste_euro.html
and
http://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/
2017-01-03/for-cheapest-power-on-earth-look-skyward-as-coal-falls-to-solar
1
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 1. Introduction
2
the older lower eﬃciency Heavy Fuel Oil (HFO) power plants. In the second IEP from CEB, the
plan is to implement more renewable energy. By doing this, the electricity production cost as well
as the emissions from the electricity production are decreased, which beneﬁts the economy and the
environment.
1.1.1
Burmeister & Wain Scandinavian Contractor
BWSC has throughout the years supplied turnkey diesel, gas and duel fuel power plant projects to
their costumers. Apart from supplying turnkey diesel power plants and be a ﬁnancial and Operation
& Maintenance (O&M) partner, BWSC is also capable of building, partly ﬁnance and operate biomass
power plants.
The diesel, gas and duel fuel power plants are primarily installed on islands system as well as smaller
countries with a limited interconnection to larger grids. For these two types of systems, diesel power
plants have been the best possibility for stable energy supply, considering cost, fuel supply and
controllability. In recent years, many diesel power plant markets have turned their focus towards
implementing renewable energy sources.
Factors that could have implied this turn of focus can
be decreasing cost of renewable energy, increased focus on sustainability and insecurity in the fuel
prices and supply in the world markets. Furthermore, the BESS price is also decreasing, causing
implementation of BESS to be economically feasible for some markets. In a report developed by the
International Electrotechnical Commission (IEC) [13], the economical feasibility of diﬀerent markets
are illustrated as seen in ﬁgure 1.1.
Figure 1.1: Proﬁtability of BESS in diﬀerent markets
From ﬁgure 1.1 it can be seen how especially island/oﬀ-grid storage can be a good investment.
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 1. Introduction
3
BWSC aims to take part and support this change that Mauritius is going through. This project will
in cooperation with BWSC investigate:
• Distribution of PMS in small electricity grids.
• Battery storage system in interaction with BWSCs existing diesel power plants.
1.2
Problem formulation
BWSC wants to investigate how a PMS in interaction with battery storage and renewable energy can
help power plants as well as small grids, to minimize power production costs and emissions, while
still securing grid stability. The aim in this project is therefore to examine how a battery energy
storage system in interaction with a PMS for smaller grids and power plants, can improve stability
and decrease power production costs. This leads to the following problem statement:
How can a battery storage solution be sized and deployed in order to maximise the use of renew-
able energy while increasing the eﬃciency of conventional power plants in Mauritius. Furthermore,
what is needed of power system control strategy and optimisation models to make the battery both
environmentally and economically feasible for Mauritius and BWSC?
To answer the problem statement, descriptions and analysis of BESS abilities are following a timeline
reaching from milliseconds to hours. Through this timeline BESS ability to contribute to stability of
the grid is covered ﬁrst, followed by the analysis of the ability to lower power production costs and
renewable curtailing. This is done by establishing a proposal to how a generation scheduling model
can be made for both a hybrid power plant and a whole power grid, and with the help of this analyse
the impact of a BESS implementation on especially power production costs.
Because of the aim of CEB to implement more renewable energy into the grid, and because of the
relationship between CEB and BWSC, the Mauritius grid is chosen as the case, where the goal is to
investigate the inﬂuence of implementation of improved PMS and BESS.
1.3
System Description
The Mauritius electricity demand is supplied by diesel, coal and bagasse power plants, a gas power
plant using kerosene, small hydro power plants and wind turbines.
Two wind farms have been
installed, providing a total capacity of approximately 39 MW.
The largest supplier of electricity is CEB that supply around 45 % of the electricity demand in
Mauritius 2. The rest of the demand is covered by Independent Power Producers (IPP). In Mauritius
there are some sugar production, where during the harvest season there is a bi-product called bagasse,
which can be used as fuel in thermal power plants. Therefore, it is mostly the sugar production
companies that builds and operates as IPPs. When bagasse is not available, coal is used as the fuel in
these power plants. The IPPs have diﬀerent Power Purchasing Agreements (PPA) with CEB. Some
2Integrated Electricity Plan 2013-2022 [p.49 [14]]
==PAGE:22==

4
of the IPPs have a "take or pay" contract with CEB, while others have a two part tariﬀmodel.
The Mauritian transmission grid is operated at 66 kV while the distribution grid is operated at 22
kV. As earlier stated, it is CEB that operates both the transmission- and distribution grid.
The present grid showing the diﬀerent voltage levels and power plants, as well as expected grid
expansions can be seen in ﬁgure 1.2.
Figure 1.2: Present and Future electricty grid
A PowerFactory ﬁle has been supplied by CEB, where all the data for lines between the generators
and substations are given. These data are used to make the models and simulations in the project.
This ﬁle can be seen in appendix A.7. Data of the diﬀerent power plants as well as historical data for
power production and demand have unexpectedly not been possible to obtain from CEB. Therefore
throughout the report, the used data is what could be extracted from the PowerFactory model
complemented with data from BWSC records and educated assumptions where needed.

2
Battery theory and parameters
Before any modelling and simulation of battery units can be performed, it is imperative to understand
the basic behaviour of batteries. This includes descriptions of important parameters as well as physical
components.
It is therefore the purpose of this chapter to introduce the main characteristics of
batteries. This chapter is meant as an introduction to batteries and for deeper understanding the
"Handbook of batteries - Third Edition"[1] is refered to.
A battery is an energy storage that utilizes a number of electrochemical units called cells in a series
or parallel combination to match a given voltage and energy requirement.
Batteries can conveniently be classiﬁed into three groups which also deﬁne their area of usage:
• Primary type of batteries is not easily charged and by such they are used and then discarded.
This type is often used in toys, remote controls etc.
• Secondary type of batteries can be recharged to their original state by forcing current through
the battery in opposite direction compared to when its being discharged.
• Reserve types are a version of primary batteries where chemical deterioration or self-discharged
is removed making them suitable for long time storage requirements.
Hence forth only the secondary type of batteries will be examined, since it is this type of battery that
is best suited as energy storage usage in the power grid.
2.1
Cell
As mentioned above the main component of a battery is the cells. It is important to understand
the basics of this component since it’s in the cells that the conversion between chemical and electric
energy is being done.
A cell is build up of three components. In order to have a clear idea of these components a discharging
process is brieﬂy explained.
• Anode is the negative electrode, which means this is the part of a cell that are releasing electrons
in an oxidized reaction.
• Cathode is the positive electrode that receives the electrons released from the anode in a reducing
electrochemical reaction.
• Electrolyte is the medium that provides the ionic conduction between the anode and cathode.
This is necessary since the electron ﬂow will appear through an external load connecting the
anode and cathode, the electrolyte then completes the electric circle by allowing ions to transfer
between the anode and cathode in the electrolyte.
Figure 2.1 illustrates how a cell functions doing charging and discharging. It should be noted that
the notation "anions" refer to negative ions and "cations" refer to positive ions.
5
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
6
(a) A cell discharging [p.24 [1]]
(b) A cell charging [p.25 [1]]
Figure 2.1: Cell components along with ion and electrons ﬂow
2.1.1
Cell voltage
One of the key parameters of a battery are the cell voltage. It can vary depending on the type of
battery, and this parameter is also used to measure the charging level of the battery. As indicated on
ﬁgure 2.1b there is a DC voltage over the cell. This voltage is determined by the standard electrode
potentials of the electrodes, in such a way that the absolute voltage drop in the anode plus the
reduction potential in the cathode is equal to the cell voltage, also known as the open cell voltage
E0. When the cell is connected to a load and conducting a current like in ﬁgure 2.1a, the cell voltage
drops. This is partly due to activation polarization and concentration polarization eﬀects on the
anode and cathode, which is beyond the scope of this report and will therefore not be discussed
further1. There is however a third and more dominant voltage drop caused by the operating current
and the internal resistance (IR) of the cell.
Figure 2.2: Voltage drop contributions at increasing operating currents [p.37 [1]]
1For further reading we refer to chapter 2 in the "Handbook of batteries" [1]
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
7
As seen on ﬁgure 2.2 the higher the operating current becomes, the lower the cell voltage, which
result in the cell being able to deliver less of its theoretical available energy, this will be discussed
further in section 2.2.
The operating voltage and current of the battery is determined by placing the cells in series or
parallel connections. The operating voltage and current is then found from ordinary circuit theory.
This operating voltage range is deﬁned as minimum and maximum voltage, which then combined with
the measured cell voltage gives an indicator of the charging level of the cell, this is further discussed
in section 2.6
2.1.2
Internal resistance
As brieﬂy mentioned in section 2.1.1 there is a voltage drop caused by the current running through
the internal resistance of the cell. Since this resistance leads to losses, it is beneﬁcial to have a low
IR. IR (Rin) is the cells opposition to the ﬂow of electric current within the cell. It appears due
to materials of construction within the cell (metal covers, conductive cathode components etc.) It
can be approximated by measuring the initial stabilized closed circuit voltage V1 by using a small
load, then measure the closed circuit voltage V2 when applying application load RL. The IR is then
calculated by:
Rin = (V1 −V2)RL
V2
(2.1)
When approximating the IR with this measure it is important to only apply the application load in
few ms, so that any polarization eﬀects is minimized.
In addition the IR is temperature dependent. It decreases at higher temperatures, resulting in a
higher cell voltage. This does not mean that by simply increasing the temperature you could increase
the performance of your battery. Higher temperature also leads to higher chemical activity which
results in a increase in self discharge, decreasing the capacity.
2.2
Speciﬁc and actual energy
The purpose of a battery is to store energy. How much energy can be stored in a battery is deﬁned
by the speciﬁc energy parameter. In other words, this parameter determines the battery’s capacity to
deliver energy, deﬁned by the properties of the corresponding cells and the amount of active anode and
cathode materials used in these cells. Each of these materials have a theoretical capacity measured
in
h
Ah
g
i
. These capacities are found from 1 gram-equivalent atomic weight of the materials and then
divided by the amount of electrons involved in the chemical reaction. A total theoretical capacity is
found by adding the capacities together for each electrode material, and multiplying with the total
weight of the reactants.
To get the speciﬁc energy of the cell is a matter of multiplying the cell voltage with the capacity of
the cell:
Energy[Wh] = V oltage[V ] · Capacity[Ah]
(2.2)
This is however not the rated energy coming out of a cell, since a cell needs more than just the two
electrodes. To name a few there are the electrolyte, container and separator. As indicated in ﬁgure
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
8
2.2 a battery is not discharging at the theoretical voltage, nor will it continue to discharge to zero
volts. This gives an "Actual speciﬁc energy" that can be seen on diﬀerent types of batteries of ﬁgure
2.3.
Figure 2.3: Theoretical and speciﬁc energy of various battery types [p.32 [1]]
The energy listed on data sheets is then the actual speciﬁc energy, since it is this energy that can be
utilized.
2.3
Eﬃciency
Through a lifetime of a battery it is being charged and discharged several times. Due to the many
cycles, it is of high importance to discuss the energy conversion eﬃciencies η. This eﬃciency deﬁnes a
percentage oﬀhow much of the energy inserted into the battery that is available through discharging.
Such an eﬃciency can be described by following equation:
η = ED
EC
[%]
(2.3)
Where EC is the total energy during charging and ED is the total energy during discharging. As
shown in previous sections there are losses regarding the IR and cell current being withdrawn or
pushed. This value then contain all the losses regarding the battery, which include chemical reaction
eﬃciencies, IR losses and self discharges. Where self discharges deﬁne a complex relationship that
comes from additional chemical reactions. These discharges defer from each battery type but are
mainly dependent on temperature, where higher temperature will increase discharges losses. Other
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
9
parameters that can inﬂuence self discharge losses are state of charge, discharge load and time.
With the relations mentioned above taken into account, it can be concluded that it is a complicated
matter to give a deﬁnitive eﬃciency, and when additional equipment bring their losses a total eﬃciency
is deﬁned. This eﬃciency is known as "Round trip eﬃciency" and will be discussed further in section
4.5.
2.4
C - rate
When charging or discharging a battery, the current is one of the parameters that aﬀect the perfor-
mance and life time the most. And to limit how "hard" you want to load the battery the C - rate
parameter is introduced.
The C rate (unit [C]) should be understood as the fraction deﬁning the time it takes to completely
discharge the battery. To better understand this concept 2 examples are brieﬂy examined.
• A battery at rated capacity of 5Ah which is charged/discharged at 1C will have a discharge
current of:
IDischarge = 1 · 5Ah = 5A
And it will take 1 hour to charge or discharge the battery.
• Same battery but operated at C rate of 0.2C will require 5 hours to be completely charged/dis-
charged, but it will do so at a lower current:
IDischarge = 0.2 · 5Ah = 1A
There can be diﬀerences in discharge and charge recommendations, but usually a battery comes with
one C rate value for both discharging and charging. This value then deﬁnes the highest recommended
operating C rate. Care is advised when operating at high C rate since high current results in higher
internal losses and more energy is converted into heat, which gives a slightly lower energy capacity
compared to operating at a lower C rate. This is illustrated on ﬁgure 2.5 in section 2.7.
2.5
Depth of discharge and State of charge
Just like the C-rate has a big inﬂuence on the performance of the battery, some battery types are
very sensitive to deep discharging. Therefore the concept of depth of discharge is introduced (also
known as DOD). It is a measure of the maximum level of discharged. A common DOD could be 80%
which means that in such a battery 20% of the capacity is unused.
2.6
State of Charge
For monitoring purposes it is easier to understand the energy available in percentage, instead of
looking at cell voltage. This leads to the calculation of State of charge (SOC). It is found by measuring
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
10
the open circuit voltage and then compare this voltage level with the minimum(completely discharged)
and maximum voltage (maximum charged) as shown in equation 2.4.
SOC =
U0 −Umin
(Umax −Umin)
(2.4)
The SOC of the battery is then found by use of the respective series/parallel ratio.
2.7
Cycle and service life
When trying to asses how much a given battery has been in operation, and thus how close it is from
reaching the end of its life, it can be misleading to simple look at its age. Depending on usage, a
battery can go through a few cycles daily to be charged/discharged constantly. To address this issue
the concept of cycles and service life is introduced.
Cycle life indicates the amount of times a battery can be charged and discharged (cycles). Where
service life is the lifetime in years that the battery can operate. Service life is dependent on the
amount of cycles, meaning that a battery going through many cycles daily will have a lower service
life compared to a battery that is seldom used.
With the cycle life being the main parameter in the battery lifetime, there are other parameters that
impact the cycle life.
DOD
DOD is aﬀecting the cycle life in a way that the deeper a battery is discharged (high DOD), the more
stress the cells endures which is leading to cell degradation. This is illustrated on ﬁgure 2.4 where
the cycle life of a lead acid battery is illustrated as a function of DOD.
Figure 2.4: DOD aﬀect on cycle life of a Hoppecke Opzv lead-acid sun-power pack [2]
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
11
C - rate
While the C - rate does not directly aﬀect cycle life, it has a great impact on the battery energy
capacity as indicated on ﬁgure 2.5.
Figure 2.5: C - rate eﬀect on capacity utilization of a Li-ion battery [p.1150 [1]]
This is important for the capacity of a battery because, discharging at a high C - rate, will exhibit
higher stress compared to low C - rates. This means that while the cycles number stay the same, the
battery will be degraded to a lower capacity much faster then if a lower C - rate is used.
Temperature
Temperature is one of the most critical parameters when it comes to batteries, which is why larger
battery systems are provided with ventilations systems for temperature control. Many battery types
lose capacity due to an increase in internal reactions at high temperatures, which also can lead to
corrosion and creation of gasses.
On the contrary at low temperatures the reactions can become sluggish and in worst case stop
altogether, with the risk of the electrolytes freezing.
Figure 2.6 shows how temperature variations can aﬀect various battery types.
==PAGE:30==

12
Figure 2.6: Temperature eﬀect on speciﬁc energy [p.580 [1]]
As seen on ﬁgure 2.6, the speciﬁc energy obtained form the diﬀerent battery types tend to be optimal
around room temperature (20 −25 ◦C).
Equivalent cycles
As mentioned earlier the usage of the battery can result in various amount of daily cycles being
performed. In addition to this the depth of each charge and discharge can vary. This leads to the
principle of equivalent cycle which is a variable calculated to indicate the amount of cycles that the
discharge / charge pattern represents. Such an calculation is shown in equation 2.5 where EC, ED, ER
represents the charging, discharging and rated energy in [kWh] respectively.
Cycles(T) =
TP
t=0

EC(t) + ED(t)

(2 · DOD · ER)
(2.5)
Equivalent cycles as shown on equation 2.5 is a tool to evaluate the amount of cycles a battery has
performed. Through equivalent cycles an assessment of batteries, that are discharged/charged around
a regular SOC, can be performed for an estimate on how long the given battery is in its cycle life.

2.8
Battery types
Through this chapter diﬀerent battery parameters has been introduced. These parameters indicates
the performance of the battery, and as seen through the various ﬁgures of this chapter, the parameters
vary depending on the type of battery. Therefore diﬀerent types of battery suits diﬀerent tasks. A
selection of battery types are brieﬂy introduced in this section, while keeping in mind that within
each type there can be several version with varying electrolyte.
• Lead-acid
This battery type is the oldest and currently most used type of battery. It uses two diﬀerent
types of lead as electrodes and a sulfuric acid solution as electrolyte.
Lead acid cells are
robust and not very sensitive to application conditions. They can easily be connected into large
battery compositions and due to their well developed technology they have some of the cheapest
installation costs.
• Lithium-ion
Li-ion batteries has a high density compared to its volume, that makes it very used in cell
phones.
Generally Li-ion batteries are divided into two categories of cathode material to compliment
lithium: (1) iron phosphate and (2) mixed metal.
The electrolyte is a polymer of lithium
carbonate. The main drawback with Li-ion batteries is in safety concerns, since the energy
density of the cells is so high they can overheat resulting in thermal runaway, ﬁre and leakage.
• Nickel based
Nickel based battery family have that in common that is uses a sort of nickel as the cathode,
where the anode can vary (cadmium, metal hydride, hydrogen or zink). The electrolyte also
varies with one use being potassium hydroxide. Nickel based batteries is mainly a part of the
alkaline family which means that the electrolyte is basic (alkaline) rather then acidic.
Nickel based batteries excels in markets that require fast charging, or operation in extreme
climates. Which also is indicated by ﬁgure 2.6 where nickel batteries exhibits low decrease in
energy as temperature deviates from room temperature.
• Sodium based
The sodium based are also known as the "Molten salt batteries" and it is mainly the NaS and
ZEBRA (Zero Emission Battery Research Activities) types. Sodium (Na) is the material of
anode and S (NaS) or NiCl (ZEBRA) is the cathode depending on the type. A solid beta
alumina tube functions as electrolyte.
Main features of sodium based batteries is high operating temperature since the electrodes needs
to be molten. But when this is achieved you receive a battery with high speciﬁc energy, constant
performance, good cycle life in harsh environments and low maintenance. Besides this ZEBRA
also develops low resistance doing faults which result in lose of single cell voltage compared to
system failure.
• Flow batteries
Unlike other types of batteries that charge/discharge the active masses of electrodes, ﬂow bat-
teries or more speciﬁc Redox ﬂow batteries stores the energy in 2 liquid electrolytes. These
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
14
electrolytes are stored in two diﬀerent tanks. By the use of pumps the electrolytes are pumped
through a electrochemical cell, where a membrane separates the electrolytes but enable ion
ﬂows.
The power of a ﬂow battery is deﬁned by the size of the electrochemical cell, but the energy is
deﬁned by the size of the tanks. Flow cells are less aﬀected by overcharge / discharge which
means that they experience notable degradation. There is however the problem that ﬂow bat-
teries rely on pumps and pipework which results in ﬂow batteries prone to leakage and it must
therefore be contained. Also the membrane is expensive and tend to corrode.
It should be noted that the values and technology descriptions shall only be used as a indication
of the battery type properties. Since research is still done in the ﬁeld of batteries, technology can
deviate from the main principle, and improvement of parameters will occur.

2.8.1
Why Li-ion battery?
In the last couple of years the Li-ion battery has been the preferred battery type for most applications,
both when it comes to batteries in electric cars, but also batteries in stationary applications like energy
arbitrage. This is seen as the main reason for the increased development of this battery type. Because
of this, the battery parameters used in the simulations in this project, will be for Li-ion batteries.
In the Li-ion battery category, there are several diﬀerent types of ion combinations that are used.
The ion combination in the Li-ion battery is needed to be determined based on the battery usage.
When a BESS is planned, it is necessary to specify what the battery should be used for, in order to
select the best battery type to this speciﬁc use.
In a stationary application the speciﬁc, which is per weight unit, energy and power are not the main
parameter for the battery, as it would have been for use in application like an electric or hybrid
car. This is because it is assumed that the weight is not a important parameter in a stationary
application.
This project describes the usage possibilities of a BESS solution ranging from grid
security and stability help to scheduling problems.
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
15
Grid Stability
If the only usage of the battery is to help with the stability of the grid, a Li-ion battery type which can
supply a high power compared with its energy capacity (C-rate » 1), is the preferred type. Batteries
that can supply power at a high C-rate are Li-ion batteries with a cathode consisting of titanate
(Li4Ti5O12), iron phosphate (LiFePO4) or manganese oxide (LiMn2O4).
Furthermore the inﬂuence of cycle losses in this type of application is high, due to the many charge/dis-
charge cycles. Therefore a Li-ion battery type with low losses has a higher priority in a grid stability
application compared with other application types.
Another solution where grid stability is the main objective is supercapacitors. As described in the
supercapacitor section 2.10, supercapacitors has the ability to quickly supply a high power and can
cope with high ﬂuctuating charge/discharge curves without degradating as much as Li-ion batteries.
Scheduling
In peak shaving or other applications where energy storage is speciﬁed as the main application, the
cost of energy is the most important parameter to pay focus. This is because the more energy the
batteries can store at a given price, the more of the peak can be shaved or stored, leading to larger
cost savings. A parameter that does not have a high importance in these types of applications are
power capabilities of the battery. When doing e.g. peak shaving, the battery capacity will be at least
the same as the power over one hour, hence the battery is charged with 1C. In cases where the peak
is shaved over several hours, the battery is charged with less than 1C. Therefore a battery that can
manage more than 1C without large degradation, is not needed.
Compared to e.g. frequency regulation application, the cycle losses in the BESS does not have a high
importance either. Typically batteries in a peak shaving or generation scheduling application is only
charged and discharged once or twice a day, which decreases the inﬂuence of the cycle loss value to
a certain extend.
As for every applications, the battery cycle life is also an important factor for the batteries in schedul-
ing application because the BESS replaces a system that usually have a longer lifetime, such as a
cable extension or a gas turbine to be used in peak load hours.
Based on the description of the important and less important parameters, a recomendation for the
Li-Ion battery type that ﬁts the power scheduling application can be given. Due to the minor cost of
the Lithium Nickel Manganese Cobalt Oxide (NMC) battery compared to the Lithium Nickel Cobalt
Aluminium Oxide (NCA) battery and other Li-ion battery types, this battery is the preferred type.
The NMC battery is also used in the Tesla Powerwall 2 which is primarily used for energy arbitrage
and peak shaving. Furthermore the NMC battery is safer than e.g. NCA batteries due to the higher
thermal stability [17]. The speciﬁc energy of NMC batteries is lower than the speciﬁc energy for
NCA batteries, but as previous stated this parameter is less important. The parameters used in
the generation scheduling, peak shaving simulations and the economical calculations are therefore
estimated parameters for the NMC battery.
2Tesla Powerwall is a plug and play household BESS developed by Tesla Inc.
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
16
2.9
Battery Degradation
As it has been stated in the battery parameter description in section 2.7, the lifetime of the battery is
inﬂuenced by cell temperature, charge/discharge current rate and DOD. In the simulations performed
later in this project, it is only the DOD and SOC that are used to estimate the battery energy capacity
degradation. This is based on the assumption that the battery cells are kept at a optimal temperature,
and that the charge/discharge current rate is kept within the limit set by the maximum C-rate.
There are two types of battery degradation. One that is caused by the time, called the "calendar
aging" and one that is caused by the charge and discharging cycles of the battery, called the "cyclic
aging".
The parameters of the NMC battery are used in the simulations to determine the battery degradation.
2.9.1
Calendar aging
When the batteries in a BESS are not charged or discharged there is a certain aging of the battery in
this steady state. The aging of a NMC battery is mainly dependent on the temperature of the cells
and the steady state SOC [p.2 [3]]. This can also be seen in ﬁgure 2.7.
(a) Temperature inﬂuence
(b) SOC inﬂuence
Figure 2.7: Temperature and SOC inﬂuence on Calender degradation [p.5 [3]]

αsei and βsei are parameters describing the charge irreversibly consumed during the formation of solid
electrolyte interphase (SEI) which is a phase just after the start of use of a new battery, where an
amount of lithium ions are used to form the SEI ﬁlm [p.3 [3]].

Li-ion batteries degradates fastest when they are new [p.3 [3]], as it can also be seen on ﬁgure 2.8.
This rate of degradation can therefore be stated from the total capacity degradation instead of the
time t. Therefore the time t in equation 2.6 is isolated, and this expression is inserted instead of t in
the diﬀerentiated equation 2.9.
Deg′
Cal(SOC, L) = 5.009 · 10−8e1.040SOC−0.520e−5.009·10−8(−1.996·107ln(−L+1)
e1.040SOC−0.520 )·e1.04SOC−0.520
(2.10)
This gives the rate of degradation per second as a function of the SOC at the speciﬁc steady state
hour and the total capacity degradation.
If it is assumed that the rate of degradation per second within one hour is the same, the degradation
at a speciﬁc steady state hour can be found by multiplying equation 2.10 with 3600.
The coherence between the rate of degradation, total degradation and SOC can be seen illustrated
in ﬁgure 2.9.
Figure 2.9: Calender degradation per hour as a function of total battery degradation and SOC
Hence it can be seen that the degradation per hour increases the larger the SOC is in the speciﬁc
hour, as it can also be seen in ﬁgure 2.8. Furthermore it can clearly be seen that the larger the total
degradation of the battery, the smaller the degradation per hour at a speciﬁc SOC.

2.9.2
Cyclic aging
When the battery cyclic degradation is described, several factors inﬂuences the cycle degradation.
Among them is the ratio between the amount of Nickel, Manganese and Cobolt used. This ratio is
often kept secret by the battery manufacturer. Depending on the Li-ion battery type, the DOD versus
number of cycles graph seen in ﬁgure 2.4, can have diﬀerent curves. Depending on the speciﬁc studies
of the NMC battery, diﬀerent relationships between the cycle life and DOD have been determined.
Diﬀerent studies describes the cycle degradation with an exponential relation [18], [3] and [7]. This
project opted for a battery degradation estimation based on [18], which can be seen in equation 2.11.
DegCyc = 2968.2 · DOD−1.699
(2.11)
In the later described power scheduling simulations, it can be seen that the DOD of each charge/dis-
charge cycle of the battery does not have the same value. Hence the degradation of each cycle as a
function of the DOD in each speciﬁc cycle is extracted. Therefore the inverse of equation is taken.
DegCyc =
1
2968.2 · DOD−1.699 = 336.9 · 10−6 · DOD1.699
(2.12)
The sum of DegCyc is set as the cyclic degradation of the battery depending on the DOD in each
cycle. In order to specify the DOD of each cycle, the parameters half- and full cycle are introduced.
The DOD in each cycle is as stated not necessarily the same. Furthermore the SOC at the start of a
cycle and the SOC at the end of a cycle are not necessarily the same either. To be able to describe
the DOD of each cycle, the Rainbow Counting Method can be used to count the number of half-
and full cycles and their speciﬁc DOD [19].The Rainbow Counting Method can be seen illustrated in
ﬁgure 2.10.
Figure 2.10: Rainbow counting method illustrated with a SOC curve during a day
The black curve is an example of a SOC curve for a given time period. The green and red line deﬁnes
respectively the charging and discharging cycles. The ﬁrst red line states a discharge cycle with a
DOD of 70%. As it can be seen, there are no charge cycles with the same DOD, and hence this cycle
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
20
is only a half cycle. A full cycle, such as the full cycle with DOD equal to 15 in ﬁgure 2.10, is obtained
when a charge- and discharge half cycle have the same value.
The degradation of each cycle can therefore be determined by equation 2.12 multiplied the cycle type
variable CType, where CType is 1 if the speciﬁc cycle is a full cycle and 0.5 if it is a half cycle.
DegCyc,c = 336.9 · 10−6 · DOD1.699
c
· CType,i
(2.13)
Index c is the speciﬁc cycle number for each cycle.
The degradation each hour is set to be the maximum of the calendar degradation and the cyclic
degradation, which is calculated in the simulations made in chapter 6.
The EOL has been considered when the total battery degradation in percentage reaches 20%. There
is a general understanding in literature that the EOL is set to be at 80% of the battery capacity [20].
Furthermore, other studies describe a large drop in capacity after this point [21].

2.10
Supercapacitors
Apart from batteries, supercapacitors are another component that can storage energy. Implemen-
tation of supercapacitors has advantages and disadvantages when compared to solutions based on
batteries.
Supercapacitors, also known as ultracapacitors and electromichanical double layer capacitors (EDLC),
diﬀers from normal capacitors in their ability to store more energy. This higher energy density is
reached by a larger surface area. Even though supercapacitors have a higher energy to weight ra-
tio compared with normal capacitors, they still have a high power to weight ratio. Supercapacitors
in general can therefore charge and discharge faster than batteries but the energy density in these
supercapacitors is smaller, meaning that they can store less energy than a similar sized battery. If
an energy storage solution is used to provide frequency support where quick charge and discharge is
needed, supercapacitors are preferred, whereas if e.g. energy arbitrage is wanted, a battery storage
solution is preferred.
As for the batteries, a capacitor has two electrodes, a positive and a negative, though in a su-
percapacitor these two electrodes consist of the same material. Between the two electrodes electric a
charge is stored. The main parameter that describes a capacitor is its capacitance, given in farads.
A supercapacitor is deﬁned by a large capacitance. The capacitance states the amount of charge Q
per voltage V.
C = Q
V [F]
(2.14)
The voltage range of one supercapacitor is fairly limited. Therefore does the value of the capacitance
determine the energy that can be stored in one capacitor, as it can be seen in equation 2.15.
E =
1
2CV 2
3600 [Wh]
(2.15)
Because of the low operating voltage of each single capacitor, they are usually serial connected in
order to increase the voltage. As it can be seen in ﬁgure 2.11 a capacitor can be expressed with
ideal components as a resistance Rs in series with an inductance in series with the equivalent of the
capacitance in parallel with the resistance Rp.
Figure 2.11: Non-ideal capacitor shown with ideal parameters
When each of the single capacitances are placed in series, the capacitance is placed in parallel, while
the serial resistances are placed in series. This can be seen in ﬁgure 2.12. Usually because of the low
impedance in a capacitor and the high resistance of RP, these are normally neglected.
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
22
Figure 2.12: serial connected non-ideal capacitor shown with ideal parameters
The downside of this though is that the total capacitance in the capacitor decreases when several
capacitors are serial connected. The capacitance of capacitors placed in series is therefore calculated
as a parallel connection which is the equation behind the decrease in capacitance. Furthermore the
serial resistances does also increase because these are in series.
Figure 2.13: Serial connected capacitors
A beneﬁt from increasing the voltage of the supercapacitor is that the maximum speciﬁc power from
and to the capacitor increases when the voltage increases, as it can be seen from equaiton 2.16.
Pmax = 1

Here ESR is the equivalent series resistance in the supercapacitor, and hence it can be seen that
the lower the ESR in the capacitor, the more power can be charged and discharged to and from the
capacitor. The ESR of a capacitor is in some litterature referred to as Rc.
The power itself is given with the power equation as a function of time.

==PAGE:41==
Chapter 2. Battery theory and parameters
23
2.10.1
Losses
During charging and discharging of a supercapacitor, there are some losses, though less losses than in
batteries, meaning that the eﬃciency of supercapacitors are greater than the eﬃciency of batteries.
The losses in a supercapacitor is due to the IR in each capacitor. Thus the higher the charging or
discharging, the higher the losses in a supercapacitor.
With regards to temperature dependency, the supercapacitors present an advantage over batteries.
The capacitance of the supercapacitors remains approximately constant at a temperature span be-
tween -40 to 70 degrees celsius. The internal resistance though is smallest between approximately 25
and 70 degrees celsius.
2.10.2
Lifetime
Unlike batteries, supercapacitors do not degradate when they are charged and discharged quickly
many times. Though the capacitance and IR in the capacitor respectively decreases and increases as a
function of time. This degradation is primarily caused by the operating voltage and temperature. Also
unlike batteries the lifetime of a supercapacitor is in theory inﬁnite. The lifetime of a supercapacitor
is therefore deﬁned as the time it takes until the capacitance of the supercapacitans to decrease a
certain amount. A supercapacitors lifetime can be decreased further if they are experiencing over
voltage, over frequency or mechanical stress.
2.10.3
Usability
Because a supercapacitor is not degradating as much as batteries when many small charge and
discharging cycles are performed, and because supercapacitors can charge and discharge very quickly
and with the same current and eﬃciency, supercapacitors are highly usable for frequency response.
Furthermore they can also be used to ﬂatten out the power output curve for renewable generation.
How quickly a supercapacitor can charge and discharge is described in its time constant. The time
constant of a supercapacitor speciﬁes the time it takes for a supercapacitor to be 63.2% charged and
36.8% discharged 4.
4Maxwell Design considerations for ultracapacitors:
http://www.maxwell.com/images/documents/technote_designinguide.pdf, [p. 3]
==PAGE:42==
3
Inverter
The inverter is the second major component in a BESS and its task is to invert direct current
(DC) from the battery into alternating current (AC) which can be delivered to the grid. Regarding
switching conversions there is three types of components to distinguish between. 1. Converter is
referring to a DC/DC conversion, 2. Inverter is DC/AC conversion and 3. Rectiﬁer is AC/DC
conversion. A BESS needs functionality of both inverting and rectifying, thus the component is called
a bi directional inverter. For simplicity when this project refers to the inverter, it is implicit the bi
directional component.
The AC side of the inverter is connected to the transformer of the BESS unit, and the DC side is
connected to the battery. Therefore the inverter also functions as charger, and recipient of the battery
discharge current. And hence needs to be able to handle the charging/discharging characteristics of
the chosen battery type.
3.1
PWM voltage inverter theory and parameters
The purpose of this section is to brieﬂy describe the functionality of an inverter and which components
it includes. While there is several types that can invert DC voltage to AC voltage, this section will not
go into diﬀerent inverter types, but mainly focus on the Single Pole Single Throw (SPST) application
of an PWM (Pulse Width Modulation) voltage inverter, since this is the most well known type used
of bi-directional components1.
Components
When it comes to the components in a inverter there are four components which is necessary to
explain for understanding the basic principle. 1. Capacitances which are located on the DC side
of the inverter. Opposite to the capacitances there are the 2. Inductances situated on the AC side
of the inverter. These inductances are also refereed to as dv/dt reactors or PWM reactors, and they
smooth out the output ﬂuctuations and decrease development of electrostatic stress. The 3. IGBT
and 4. Diode is connected in an anti-parallel manner and represents a SPST switch as shown in
ﬁgure 3.1.
1Fundamentals of Power Electronics [p.687 [4]]
24
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 3. Inverter
25
Figure 3.1: Current-birectional two-quadrant SPST switch using a IGBT [p.68 [4]]
A SPST as shown on ﬁgure 3.1 works in a way that when a positive voltage is present on the Gate
(G) then current i can ﬂow from the collector to the emitter resulting in a "positive current" and the
transistor is conducting. The direction of the diode ensures that a "negative current" can ﬂow through
the diode when the voltage v becomes negative and the diode is forward biased. These diodes are
also refereed to as free-wheeling diodes.
3.1.1
PWM waveform
Inverting DC voltage into AC voltage is a matter of clever switching the SPST’s. This is done by
utilizing a PWM signal which is inserted on the gate of the IGBT. Such a PWM signal is created by
comparing a sinusoidal reference voltage Vref to a delta shaped voltage waveform Vh. The reference
voltage is swinging at grid frequency fout, while Vh is switching at much higher frequency fsw, also
known as switching frequency. Thus the PWM signal becomes positive when Vref is greater than Vh,
and vice versa negative when Vh is greater than Vref. This results in a square wave signal, switching
at fsw. The period 1/fsw of this square wave contains a time where the voltage is positive, and for
the remaining time the voltage is negative. This time period is known as duty cycle, where the ratio
of time with positive voltage and negative voltage is varying with the sinusoidal Vref. For better
understanding, ﬁgure 3.2 shows a PWM signal (V
′
out) with same frequency as Vh.
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 3. Inverter
26
Figure 3.2: Single phase module with corresponding voltage and current waveforms [p.277 [5]]
3.1.2
Current waveform
Figure 3.2 shows how a AC current iout(t) is generated, from a DC voltage input by the use of two
SPST’s and a PWM signal. The bottom SPST is receiving the complemented PWM signal, compare
to the top SPST. And the AC current is taken from the output of the two SPST, where it consists of
the transistor current IT and diode current ID.
By comparing IT and ID with the PWM signal on ﬁgure 3.2, it can be seen that when the PWM
signal is positive then IT ﬂows, due to a positive voltage over the SPST. The bottom SPST sees a
negative voltage, and due to the current direction on the output the bottom IGBT is blocked. When
the complemented PWM signal becomes positive then the diode is able to conduct, and the current
ID ﬂows.
The shape of currents IT and ID can be explained by the PWM inductances (located at the output).
Because when the top SPST is conducting, the voltage over the inductor becomes higher and, a
charging of the inductance occurs, resulting in and increasing current waveform as indicated on IT.
However when the bottom diode is conducting, the voltage over the inductance becomes lower, and
it begins to discharge which then is seen as the current ID. Since current through an inductance cant
change instantaneously due to VL(t) = L · diL(t)
dt
the current waveform becomes a summation of the
waveform IT and ID and thus resembles a sinusoidal shown as iout(t).
What is not shown on ﬁgure 3.2 is that when iout(t) crosses zero and becomes negative, the operation
switches and it is the bottom transistor and top diode that are operating, but in same principle as
before.
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 3. Inverter
27
DC /three phase AC inverter
The BESS operates at a three phase system, which means that a three phase inverter is needed. Such
an inverter is achieved by connecting three single phase modules from ﬁgure 3.2 and combine them
in three parallel connections. The three phases are taken at the output of each stage. Such a three
phase inverter is shown on ﬁgure 3.3.
Figure 3.3: 3 phase voltage source inverter/rectiﬁer [p.687 [4]]
In order to create positive sequence, three diﬀerent PWM signals are utilized. Where the three PWM
signals are phase shifted 120◦from each other.
3.1.3
Rectiﬁer mode
So far the operation descriptions have only covered inverter mode and thus the BESS discharging.
There is, however, also the need for charging the battery as well, which will then be done by rectiﬁer
mode.
Considering the inverter in ﬁgure 3.3, it resembles that of a boost type three phase AC
to DC PWM rectiﬁer.
Since the voltage cannot changed immediately over an capacitor due to
iC(t) = C · dvC(t)
dt
charging and discharging the capacitor is utilized to create the desired DC voltage.
This is done by use of the PWM inductances L which will be charged when the corresponding bottom
SPST is on, allowing current through that SPST and back through the 2 other bottom SPST diodes.
When the bottom SPST is oﬀ, the current will ﬂow through the top diode and discharge the PWM
inductance and charge the capacitance.
When operating in negatively half cycle the operation occurs with the top transistor and the bottom
diode.
To ensure controlled operation of the inverter is imperative that the voltage over the capacitor is
higher than the AC line peak to peak voltage VL,pk. If this is not respected the inverter will operate
as a normal diode bridge rectiﬁer at all values of instantaneous AC voltage. This dependency is
illustrated on ﬁgure 3.4.
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 3. Inverter
28
Figure 3.4: DC link voltage of a PWM rectiﬁer [p.12-37 [6]]
Negative polarization of the diode is obtained as long as the DC voltage is higher than any momen-
tarily AC voltage. As longs as this is true the rectiﬁer operations can be controlled through the PWM
operation of the transistors.
3.1.4

To deﬁne the desired DC voltage, the modulation index Dm is introduced. This index is deﬁned as:
Dm =

3.1.5
Losses
As for any high power devices used in the electricity sector, losses are of a great importance. Since
the inverter is the link between grid and batteries, the losses generated on the inverter and hence the
eﬃciency of the inverter will play a role in both, charging and discharging cycles. This section will
therefore highlight the main losses that occurs in the inverter.
2Semikron -Application Manual Power Semiconductors [p 278 [5]]
3J.Dixon - Three phase controlled rectiﬁers [p.12-48 [6]]
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 3. Inverter
29
Pcond
The ﬁrst losses encountered in the inverter is in the PWM inductance, where the so called conduction
losses appears (Pcond). These losses represents copper losses due to the current and natural resistance
of the copper wire, and core losses due to hysteresis and eddy currents in the magnetic core of the
inductance.
Pfixed
Fixed losses covers other frequency independent losses like the amount of power required to operate
the control circuit, which generate the PWM signal controlling the inverter.
Psw
Switching losses are the energy lost during turn on and oﬀof the switches as shown in equation 3.3
Psw = 1
Ts
Z
pA(t)dt
→
Psw = (Won + Woff) · fsw
(3.3)
The energies Won and Woff are deﬁned as the switching losses for each duty cycle operation, as shown
in ﬁgure 3.5.
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 3. Inverter
30
Figure 3.5: IGBT ON and OFF waveforms [p.51 [5]]
The three plots on ﬁgure 3.5 shows the Gate - Emitter voltage (VGE) on top, the Collector current
(iC) in the middle and the Collector - Emitter voltage (VCE) at the bottom.
Won
When the IGBT is starting to turn on, the PWM signal (VGE) starts to increase, consequently when
the threshold voltage VGE(th) is reached, the collector current iC starts to rise (t1 −t2). At t2 the
diode is turned oﬀresulting in a drop in VCE down to almost saturated levels. When no current is
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 3. Inverter
31
ﬂowing back through the diode the current through the IGBT falls to the given load current IL. The
last phase (t3 −t4) is called the saturation area. In this phase the IGBT reaches completely turn on
state when the n−drift area (Collector) is ﬂooded with minority carriers4.
The losses Won occurs in the time period (t1 −t4) where current is building up and there still is a
voltage drop over the IGBT until it becomes saturated and the low on state voltage appears.
Woff
During switch oﬀof a phenomenon called "current tailing" occurs. This phenomenon is showed on
ﬁgure 3.5 at time steps (t1 −t4) on the end of the time scale.
First the VGE receives a turn oﬀsignal and the voltage starts to drop. When it reaches the voltage
VGE(pl) at t1, VCE starts to build up until the diode becomes forward biased. When this happens the
current starts to switch from the IGBT to the diode, meaning that the collector current iC starts to
decrease at t2.
Parasitic inductances cause VCE to overshoot at t3 and due to high amount of minority carriers in
the n−drift area, a backward injection current occurs resulting in the slowly decreasing "current tail"
seen from time (t3 −t4). Just like switch on losses (Won) the switch oﬀlosses (Woff) are determined
by the presence of a current simultaneously with the VCE.
The time intervals shown on ﬁgure 3.5 is a bit misleading regarding how long it takes to switch
on ton and switch oﬀtoff. These times can oﬀcourse vary with example values being ton a couple of
hundred nanoseconds and toff 4 - 5 times higher.
3.1.6
Switch frequency fsw
As described above, there are losses during switch on and oﬀ. These losses occur every time the
IGBT switch states and hence they rely on the switching frequency fsw. This is reﬂected on equation
3.3 where fsw is included as a multiplier. This also means that higher switching frequency, results in
larger switching losses Psw.
Figure 3.6 shows typical frequency / eﬃciency dependencies of a full load inverters. A variable called
fcrit is also shown, this is the so called critical frequency and it illustrates the frequency value where
the switching losses exceeds other inverter losses, and it is often used as a rough upper limit.
4For further elaboration on structure and functional principle of an IGBT refer to [p. 43-46 [5]]
==PAGE:50==

32
Figure 3.6: Switching frequency fsw as a function of eﬃciency η [p.101 [4]]
From ﬁgure 3.6 it becomes clear that a lower fsw is to be preferred when it comes to eﬃciency. It
should be noted however that reducing fsw would result in an increase in weight and size of the
passive energy storage components. This result in guidelines for standard IGBT modules as shown
in table 3.1:

3.1.7
Powerfactor control
By controlling the PWM signal, active and reactive power output can also be controlled. The voltage
and current on the grid side is measured, and then the reference signal (Vh(t)) is modiﬁed with a
phase shift. The corresponding inverter output will then also become lagging/leading depending on
the desired output. By introducing a feedback, the reference signal can be modiﬁed until the desired
output is reached.
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 3. Inverter
33
3.2
Inverter setup
Inverters used for energy storage systems can be connected in two ways, Single-stage (ﬁgure 3.7a)
and Multi-stage (ﬁgure 3.7b). The diﬀerence between the two ways lies in the DC/DC converter
implemented in the multi-stage.
(a) Single-stage [22]
(b) Multi-stage (two-stage) [22]
Figure 3.7: Types of inverters used in BESS applications
The single-stage inverter is more eﬃcient due to less components, but presents the disadvantages of
DC voltage on the inverter ﬂoating due to the voltage behaviour of the battery. The multi-stage con-
trols the DC voltage through the DC/DC converter, which also beneﬁts with reduced voltage ripple
and harmonic distortion, however as mentioned earlier it comes with increased losses and hardware
costs.
Both types have a ﬁlter before the transformer in order to limit harmonics and switching noise for
increased power quality.
3.3
Filter
Filters represents the last component in the inverter chapter since a ﬁlter is often located at the
output of the inverter. It is an individual component, but it is often supplied with the inverter and
installed in the same rack. An output ﬁlter located on the AC side of the inverter as shown on ﬁgure
3.7a and 3.7b is used to reduce the output current ripple on the grid along with given switching noise,
so the inverter complies with grid codes. [22] These grid codes can be diﬀerent depending on the
TSO.
If need be it is possible to implement a DC ﬁlter before the inverter. The DC ﬁlter circuit is a high
pass ﬁlter that protects the batteries from harmonic current injection and reduces the voltage ripple.
[23]
==PAGE:52==
4
Battery Energy Storage System
Battery Energy Storage System (BESS) is an energy storage containing batteries that can be con-
trolled to perform diﬀerent grid services. It is a complete product ready for grid connection, meaning
that it contains the needed equipment for safe operations on a given grid.
A single line diagram showing the main components in a BESS is shown in ﬁgure 4.1.
Figure 4.1: Single line diagram representation of a BESS [7]
This chapter will introduce these components along with which services a BESS can provide.
4.1
Battery racks
The power source in a BESS is the battery cells which has been introduced in chapter 2. These cells
are connected in a combination of series and parallel to match the wanted power and voltage output.
Practically the cells are gathered in shelf like modules which then are inserted into a rack. This
arrangement facilitates maintenance and replacement.
4.2
Battery Management System
The battery management system (BMS) is responsible for managing the cells in the given battery.
The functionality of such a system can vary depending on the implementation, but features can
include:
• Monitoring the cells voltage and temperature.
• Protecting the cells from overcharging.
• Maximizing battery performance by equalization.
• Reporting battery state parameters.
34
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 4. Battery Energy Storage System
35
Protection of the cells is done by ensuring operation within the safe operating voltage limits of
the cells. Unsafe operation can occur when multiple cells are connected, resulting in a given total
nominal open circuit voltage. But if the voltage over a individual cells vary from the neighbouring cell,
then charging to the nominal open circuit voltage of the battery can result in overcharging of given
cells, which could result in explosion or leakages. Such a diﬀerence in cell voltage can occur due to
temperature diﬀerences resulting in an increase in IR and therefore diﬀerence in charging/discharging
rates.
The BMS prevents this by measuring individual cell voltages, and if a certain cell voltage is too
high, this will be individually discharged through a regulator (equalization). The BMS is mainly
monitoring and its control functionality is automatic and internal. For further information regarding
the functionality of a BMS we refer to Andrea, Davide Battery management systems for large lithium-
ion battery packs [24].
4.3
Inverter
Chapter 3 introduced the inverter as a component and described the dynamic behaviour and func-
tionality of the inverter. This section focus on the inverter as a component in the BESS, meaning
which set-ups inverters can be installed in and how they are controlled.
4.3.1
Control of multiple DC sources
For optimized renewable integration, it is often used to connect several DC sources to the same in-
verter. This could be a hybrid battery system with two diﬀerent types of batteries, PV (Photovoltaic)
production, fuel cell integration or simply several battery stacks to increase total BESS energy. Such
multiple DC sources set-ups can be seen on ﬁgure 4.2.
(a) Several DC/DC converters
(b) Several DC/AC inverters
Figure 4.2: Combinations of converters/inverters when connecting several battery packs
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 4. Battery Energy Storage System
36
Figure 4.2a utilizes several DC/DC converters connected to one DC/AC converter in a variation of
multi stages. Where ﬁgure 4.2b uses the single stage principle with only DC/AC inverter.
Which set-up used can be depending on speciﬁc grid requirements or a matter of utilizing simpler
and cheaper stock inverters and a more expensive transformer. Designs like these change through
time and can vary from diﬀerent manufactures.
4.3.2
Control modes
Figure 4.2a illustrates a "Master Controller" which controls the set point of the inverter and thus the
power ﬂows. As discussed in section 3.1 the inverters are controlled through the PWM signal, but
the control input can vary depending on the preferred choice.
There are 4 diﬀerent modes that the inverter control after.
• AC Current (P,Q)
This is standard for a production unit on a grid to deliver decided amount of active and reactive
power.
• AC Voltage (V,F)
Island mode where the purpose of the mode is to maintain grid voltage and frequency within
the given limits.
• DC Current (IDC)
When connected to the grid the purpose of this mode is to control the charging/discharging
current for given C-rates.
• DC Voltage (VDC)
The focus of this mode is to manage battery conditioning, to ensure long life time and no deep
discharging.
The importance of these control modes for the inverter is tied up with the BESS units overall control
system, that can operate in more general operation modes. These are explained further in section
4.6.
4.4
Additional equipment
In order for the BESS to be a complete product ready for grid connection it requires additional
equipment besides the battery and inverter. This equipment are kept out of the scope of this project.
Nevertheless this section will brieﬂy introduce the additional equipment.
Step up transformer
The step up transformer is located at the output of the BESS and it is responsible for transforming
the voltage up to the voltage of the grid where it is located.
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 4. Battery Energy Storage System
37
Switchgear and protection equipment
This section includes circuit breakers, switch disconnecter, protective relays and fuses to ensure safe
and reliable grid connections. A switch disconnecter is located on each side of the inverter, with a
DC fuse located at the inverter DC side as well. The AC breaker is located after the AC switch
disconnecter and before the transformer as shown as the second last component on ﬁgure 4.1.
Temperature control
Although not included on ﬁgure 4.1, temperature control should be mentioned as well. As discussed
in chapter 2, the temperature is a critical parameter for battery performance, and if temperature be-
comes to high the battery will endure higher degradation. Additionally the transformer and inverter
also require cooling. Due to the importance of temperature control, a BESS unit is often divided
into several compartments where the temperature can be controlled individually. The battery com-
partment is often held at room temperature, while the transformer and converter compartments are
further cooled.
4.5
Round trip eﬃciency
The round trip eﬃciency is the overall eﬃciency of the BESS unit. It is calculated in similar fashion
as the battery eﬃciency, by examining how much of the energy charged from the grid is available
when discharging. This eﬃciency is measured at the point of connection (POC) i.e at the output of
the step up transformer.
It has been discussed in a earlier section that the energy path is exposed to losses when the power ﬂow
in both directions. These losses are not equally big, since batteries and inverters exhibits diﬀerent
eﬃciencies depending on the BESS being charged or discharged
A round trip eﬃciency takes these diﬀerences into account, however this value is meant as an indica-
tion. Since other parameters like mode of operation and age would play a role as well.
4.6
BESS control system
Since BESS functions as a complete unit on the grid, it also needs to exhibit complete functionality
of a generating/load unit. Therefore diﬀerent parameters and control of the entire BESS are gathered
into a control system, that then can communicate with a remote control centre. [25]
Such control system is responsible for controlling the switchgear while monitoring the protection
equipment, and reporting dangerous behaviour. It gathers parameters from the BMS system, so it
can report the battery condition. Finally it requires instructions, operating mode and P and Q set
points, that are given locally or remote. These set points are forwarded to the inverter control, which
converts the set points into a PWM signal in order to achieve the desired P and Q grid contributions,
or when required turn the process around to charge up the battery storages.
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 4. Battery Energy Storage System
38
4.6.1
Usage possibilities
Depending on the the speciﬁcations of the given installation, a BESS can operate in various modes,
which could include: [26]
• Peak shaving
Charging the battery at periods of excess power, and discharge when there is a high demand
(load peaks) thus reducing the load on conventional power plants.
• Spinning reserves
Be at service with active power within 10 seconds of shortfall on the grid.
• Load levelling
Similar to peak shaving but with a longer time span, meaning that instead of only removing
peaks the battery will discharge in longer time periods and thus postponing otherwise invest-
ments in grid upgrades or generating capacity.
• Frequency regulation
Assist the grid with stabilizing the frequency by charging when frequency is high and discharging
at low frequencies.
• Voltage support
Similar to frequency regulation, but with stabilization eﬀect on the voltage by the use of reactive
power.
• Capacity ﬁrming
Smoothing the output of renewable generation like wind and PV, by stabilizing ramp rates and
eliminating fast and big voltage swings.
Further usage possibilities include ancillary service and arbitrage, if there is an electricity market. In
Mauritius it is CEB that controls the market, hence the economy behind a battery storage solution
needs to be treated diﬀerently than e.g. in Denmark. This is due to the fact that a business case can
be build on providing ancillary services, since these services are paid for when a electricity market is
present.
4.7
Simulations
For better understanding the strengths and weakness of a BESS, simulations and calculations are
performed. The purpose of these is to investigate the dynamic behaviour of a BESS with regard to
Power quality and Control and regulation.
The simulations covered are based on the Danish TSO Energinet.dk consultation document [10] re-
garding implementation of BESS. The reasoning behind the use of this document, is that it gives
some concrete guidelines for a BESS, and guidelines for BESS implementation in Mauritius have not
yet been developed by CEB. Also it is assumed that if a BESS can live up to danish standards, then
it should suﬃce for Mauritius.
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 4. Battery Energy Storage System
39
Power quality
The ﬁrst simulations investigates the quality of the power delivered to the grid. In concrete following
topics are investigated:
• DC content
• Asymmetry
• Flicker
• Harmonic content
• Short circuit contributions
Control and regulation
After power qualities has been shown and discussed the automatic control and regulation capabilities
of a BESS will be investigated in following topics:
• Frequency control
• Q regulation
• Capacity ﬁrming
==PAGE:58==
5
Dynamic Behaviour
The intentions of this chapter is to discuss and simulate the dynamic behaviour a BESS exhibits,
in order to give a better understanding of what to expect of a BEES in operation. This is done by
modelling a BESS in PowerFactory and then performing various simulations.
5.1
PowerFactory Model
Before any simulations can be performed, the model needs to be implemented. The model is based
on an application example [27] developed by DIgSILENT.1 The purpose of this section is to explain
how this template works.
5.1.1
Grid components
The BESS template contains two grid components that when conﬁgured correctly represents the
behaviour of a BESS. These components are a (1) PWM inverter and a (2) DC voltage source.
These two components are connected together through a DC bus, while the output of the BESS unit
is connected to low voltage (LV) bus. This conﬁguration is shown in ﬁgure 5.1.
Figure 5.1: BESS simpliﬁcation into two grid components
1An existing model is chosen instead of developing one, since the goal of this project is to investigate the usage
possibilities of a BESS. If a model was to be developed, the project would go into a direction of programming in
PowerFactory, where PowerFactory is merely meant as a tool for illustrative purpose.
40
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
41
It should be noted that this template does not include step up transformer or any switchgear. The
step up transformer is inserted later in the general simulation grid scenario, and for switchgear the
build in cubicles in PowerFactory is used.
5.1.2
Composite frame
The composite frames deﬁnes the control dynamics of a BESS, which means that the components
shown in ﬁgure 5.1 is modelled by a composite frame. A composite frame contains slots that are
given a type, so the content of the slot must match the type of the slot.
An example could be
power measurements, which would have a type "StaPqMea". This would require the user to insert a
measurement module with the type "StaPqMea" else the compiler would generate an error. Inputs
and outputs are generated for each slots. The module in the slot can either be predeﬁned code blocks
or manually inserted equations.
Main controller frame
The overall composite frame is the main composite frame and it is shown on ﬁgure 5.2 and it contains
all the general slots used in the BESS control.
Figure 5.2: Composite frame of the BESS Control system

slots named "AC-Voltage, Frequency Measurement and PQ Measurement" supply the needed mea-

5.2
BESS modelling
The BESS unit that will be implemented in the PowerFactory model is based on a technical proposal
for a 5.2 MW Battery Energy Storage System from ABB A/S. [11]
Where the main components needed for the PowerFactory model are the batteries and inverters.
Battery
To give the BESS unit the needed power capability a large amount of battery cells are required. The
proposal from ABB was respected and the choice of battery are Lithium ion batteries from LG Chem.
As a BESS often is a containerized solution, the battery is divided in cells, modules and racks which
then in total represents the battery part of the BESS unit. The key components and parameter values
of the modelled BESS unit are summarised in table 5.3.

better suited for long output duration energy applications. So the design could with relative ease be
modiﬁed for such an application. This would require switching to another cell type with a higher
energy density, which then would come at the cost of the C-rate.

5.3
Test grid
For the dynamic simulations a small test grid is designed.
The test grid is used instead of the
Mauritius grid, because the purpose of this chapter is to show the capabilities of a BESS unit. By
using a simpler grid it becomes easier to observe and understand BESS behaviour. Whereas in the
Mauritius grid results would contain the dynamic behaviour of the grid, which can make it diﬃcult
to distinguish contributions.
The components of the test grid are listed below and the grid itself can be seen on ﬁgure 5.5.
• The 5.2 MW BESS unit as described in section 5.2
• A 4 MVA 11/0.415 kV Dyn11 unit transformer. [29] Well known and common transformer
design. For simulations purpose a rating factor of 1.3 has been applied to match the 5.2 MW
of the BESS.
• A general PQ load
• Two lines of the type Naysey 3x95rm /16 6/10 kV in parallel. These are simulated as cables
and have been chosen from the global library of PowerFactory.
• A 37.4 MVA synchronous machine operating at 11 kV. The model for this machine has been
extracted from the Mauritius grid where it is operating in Fort Victoria power station.
When doing the diﬀerent simulations the magnitude of the general load is set to match a wanted sce-
nario, then set points of the BESS are determined for the sought initial conditions. The synchronous
machine operates as the reference machine and picks up deﬁcit in production.

5.5
Short circuit and overload current
The purpose of short circuit calculations it to determine Maximum and Minimum values. Maxi-
mum current values is needed for determining the capacity and rating of other electrical equipment,
where minimum values is used in dimensioning fuses or other protective devices. This section will
expose the test circuit to a three phase short circuit since it is considered to be the most severe
fault,3 and therefore resulting in the highest current. When calculating the minimum short circuit
currents, IEC60909[30] states that contributions from power stations with full size converter (e.g.
wind, photovoltaic or BESS) shall be neglected. This results in minimum short circuit current being
of no interest in this section.
To achieve the highest short circuit contribution from a BESS, the impedance between the BESS and
the fault needs to be as low as possible. Therefore the short circuit locations is chosen to be at LV
busbar on ﬁgure 5.5 between the inverters and the unit transformer.
In the calculations, the BESS is seen as a power station unit along with the transformer. The output
of this investigations are:
• I
′′
K Initial symmetrical short circuit current [ARMS]
• IK Steady state short circuit current [ARMS]
• S
′′
K Initial symmetrical short circuit power [VA]
• iDC Decaying DC component [A]
• ip Peak short circuit current [A]
Short circuit calculations can be done in several ways e.g. Complete method, ANSI, IEC60909 among
others. In this report the method of choice is the IEC60909 protocol.
3Non simultaneous short circuits can lead to higher currents, but this is beyond the scope of this report.

explanation is located in appendix A.1:
