Short circuit discussion
IEC60909 states that contributions of a power station units with a full size inverter can be neglected
when the contribution is less than 5% of I
′′
K without the inverter contribution. In this scenario IskPF
==PAGE:72==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
54
represents
I
′′
skP F
I′′
kT
· 100 = 20.29% of the initial short circuit current. It is therefore required to include
contributions from the BESS.
Energinet.dk [10] does not include any short circuit contribution requirements. There are however
some requirements of withstand voltage during short circuit on the grid.
Short circuit of the inverter
During a fault IGBTs and free wheeling diodes can be damaged due to thermal destruction caused by
a to high power dissipation, or due to an over voltage which can occur with the fault currents. A short
circuit current through an IGBT can reach values up to IC = 10 · Inom, which would quickly push
the chip towards thermal breakdown. To ensure this doesn’t happen the inverter uses 2 protection
circuit to save the IGBT’s. First it limits the IC by clamping the gate voltage of the IGBT with the
help of a capacitance connected to the gate and thus discharging the gate. When the current has
been reduced it is time to turn of the transistor. This has to be done slowly to reduce the voltage
overshoot. By connecting another capacitances in parallel with the clamping capacitances, the gate
voltage is eﬀectively lowered until the IGBT is switched oﬀ.
DC side short circuit
If the short circuit occurs in the DC side then a battery delivers a short circuit current which increases
towards a peak value ipB, calculated from the open circuit voltage EB and the resistance RBBr (sum
of IR and external circuit resistance).[31]
ipB =
EB
RBBR
(5.30)
The rise time of this current is dependent on the ratio of the batteries (X
R ) and the short circuit is
switched oﬀwith the use of DC fuses. The inverter will conduct short circuit current through the
diodes which will be as high as IFRM = 2 · IFnom which then in most cases will corresponds to the
rated current of the inverter. A fuse is then responsible for switching oﬀthis current, where the
switch is dimensioned by the surge peak current of a sinusoidal half wave (current wave lasting 10 ms
at - 50Hz)6
Unless measures has been taken to keep the capacitor at a ﬂoating state, the capacitor will be
discharged into the short circuit as well. This will create a instantaneous high peak current, but it’s
duration is in the matter of µs so it does not aﬀect the ratings of protection devices.
BESS overload capabilities
BESS short circuit contribution to a fault on the grid is in data sheets listed under overload capabili-
ties, for a ABB turnkey product (PowerStore)[32] these values are IC = 2·Inom for a time duration of
2s assuming 75% pre-load, otherwise it is IC = 1.5·Inom for 30s. These values support the calculations
done in this section, and it can therefore be assumed that similar time limits would apply for the
chosen BESS system.
6Maximum ratings of freewheeling diodes - Surge forward current IF SM [p. 162 [5]]
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
55
While the consultation document does not contain any current limits, it does however contain time
durations that a BESS should stay connected during a fault, for a three phase short circuit, this
duration is 150ms7. With the assumptions done in the section it can therefore be concluded that the
chosen BESS, would comply with the requirements.
While short circuit contribution from a BESS is not great, it can become quite important when tra-
ditional units are being replaced with renewable generation. When this happens the general fault
current on the grid will decrease, and if it becomes too low a potential dangerous situation where a
fault is not detected and interrupted could occur. A scenario like that could result in new grid wise
short circuit calculations and maybe even redesign of protection equipment.
5.6
Harmonics
Inverters are harmonic polluters of an AC power system.
Since the society is incorporating an
increasing amount of electrical equipment harmonics are a signiﬁcant problem.
A harmonic current is current wave that swings at a frequency multiple of the fundamental frequency.
For better understanding of harmonic currents, it can be expressed as fourier series:
i(t) = I0 +
∞
X
h=1
Ihcos(hωt −θn)
(5.31)
I0 is the DC component and h denotes the given harmonic number, where h = 1 is the fundamental
with an angular velocity of ω = 2πf. The output current i(t) contains the sum of all these components
which means that if some harmonics besides the fundamental has an high amplitude (Ih) an distorted
output current is produced. With a distorted output current through an impedance, a distorted
voltage is induced as well.
Harmonics occur when current is ﬂowing through a non linear load, such as, switching mode power
supplies (SMPS) and inverters. As explained in chapter 3 inverters are pulsating current by the
use of SPST’s, and these pulses contains many frequencies. Regarding harmonics the asymmetrical
waveforms or "even" harmonics are not considered, these are also refereed to as DC components.
Inverters are pulsating at both polarities and by such they are mainly creating symmetrical harmonics
or "uneven".
Problems created by harmonics include among others:
• Unsafe neutral connections since the sum of harmonics divisible by 3 can be found in the neutral
wire.
• Heating and reduction of life in transformers.
• Malfunctioning of certain power system protection elements, due to unforeseen high currents
and voltages.
Due to the potential problems caused by harmonics, the restrictions regarding harmonic distributions
are a point of interest in relation to inverters.8
7Multiple fault in the collective power grid [p. 31 [10]]
8The theory regarding harmonics are mainly obtained from [8] and Chapter 16. [4]
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour

Equation 5.32 and 5.33 show how to calculate THD and PWHD current factors. Since a harmonic
current is causing a harmonic voltage Uh a THD factor can easily be redeﬁned for calculating THD
harmonic distortion in a voltage signal.
Standards deﬁne the allowed harmonic content as a percentage of the fundamental. This is done for
the characteristic harmonics, since it is these harmonics that an inverter in steady state operation
are producing. Characteristic harmonics is deﬁned by equation 5.34:
h = np ± 1
(5.34)
From equation 5.34 the order of characteristic harmonics (h) is deﬁned by an integer (n) and the
number of pulses per cycle (p). For a three phase inverter with six SPST’s the equivalent pulses per
cycle would be p = 6.
The last parameter used to ﬁnd the requirements that the inverter is subjected to is the short circuit
ratio SCR. This ratio deﬁnes the relation between short circuit power at POC Sk and BESS apparent
power rating Sn as shown in equation 5.35
SCR = Sk

(5.35)

5.6.2
Harmonic Mitigation
To mitigate the harmonic currents the chosen inverter (ESI-I [9]) is utilizing line reactors and an
active ﬁlter.
Line reactors
Line reactors is simply a reactor connected on each phase on the output of the inverter. Due to the
behaviour of an inductor when a current is ﬂowing, an induced voltage appears across the terminal in
the opposite direction of the applied voltage. This has the eﬀect that current ﬂuctuations are limited,
thus harmonics are being smoothed out. Installing line reactors does however introduce additional
copper losses and a voltage drop on the output.
Line reactors usually comes in sizes of 3% to 5%, where the % rating is relative to the load impedance
at full load. A 5% line reactor is more eﬀective in ﬁltering out the harmonics, but it does cause a
higher voltage drop. For this reason, inverters at relatively high voltage ratings usually go with lower
line reactors.9 By observing the input voltages at MTE - RL Line /Load Reactors brochure [33] it is
assumed that the line reactor in this simulations is at 3%.
Active ﬁlter
An active ﬁlter is connected as a shunt ﬁlter on the AC side of the inverter. It is functioning by
measuring the "distortion current" wave after ﬁltering out the fundamental current. It produces a
current waveform containing the undesired harmonics that are to be ﬁltered, with the ratio of the
amount they should be reduced. This waveform is injected in anti-phase to the inverter output. This
principle is indicated in a single line diagram shown on ﬁgure 5.7.
Figure 5.7: Single line diagram showing a typical connection of active ﬁlter [p.19 [8]]
Since the active ﬁlter is functioning by creating a speciﬁc current waveform from the measured
waveform, it is functioning by controlling a PWM inverter equipped with a DC capacitor and a
switching ripple ﬁlter10.
9This tendency has been extracted from MTE - RL Line /Load Reactors brochure [33]
10Indicated on ﬁgure 21 [p.38 [9]], this ﬁlter is a passive ﬁlter eliminating switching ripples.
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
58
The way the active ﬁlter in the ESI-I inverter functions is by deﬁning curve levels for a given harmonic
order, the active ﬁlter then ﬁlters the given harmonic current down to the curve setting level as shown
in ﬁgure 5.8.
Figure 5.8: Active ﬁlter ﬁltering to curve setting [p.105 [9]]
The levels shown on ﬁgure 5.8 can be thought as a minimum requirements regarding the harmonic
content. The active ﬁlter is capable of additional ﬁltering, but it might not be in the interest of the
user since it may be of the expense of reactive compensation. This is solved by mode selection, where
the highest priority of the inverter is to supply the active power set point. Second highest priority
is ﬁltering the harmonics down to curve level, then a user deﬁned mode selects if maximum ﬁltering
(down to zero) or reactive compensation should come next in the priority list.11
An active ﬁlter is an eﬀective way to mitigate harmonics, but it does comes with the added cost of
an additional controlled inverter along with complex measuring to determine the needed harmonic
counteracting required.
5.6.3
Calculations and simulation
When it comes to harmonics in PowerFactory the method for creating harmonic injections is simply
manual entry in % for the characteristic harmonic order from h = 5, 7, 11...25. This however would
not include the impact of an active ﬁlter, which is why the simulations are done using MATLAB
instead.
The calculation procedure used is based on the Fast Fourier Transformation (FFT) which is a quick
algorithm to perform a Discrete Fourier Transformation (DFT). When the current waveform is di-
vided into individual harmonics, an amplitude of harmonic distortion is applied to the characteristic
harmonics. An inverse FFT is performed to recreate the waveform in the time domain after which
the result can be inspected.
A simulated steady state current waveform at full load is extracted from PowerFactory and harmonic
distortion is applied in Matlab. After that the active ﬁlter is inserted by copying the waveform and
removing the fundamental, inserting ﬁltering percentage and add it to the distorted waveform in
anti phase. Finally the output waveform, after the active ﬁltering contribution has been added, is
inspected.
11Detailed inverter modes descriptions can be found in [p.105 [9]]
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
59
DFT
A DFT12 is a tool to analyse a function fd(t) which contains N points. This means that the function
is operating in discrete time, where each point is sampled with a sample time Ts. The DFT gives the
tools to determine a complex trigonometric polynomial that interpolates at regularly spaced points:
xn = 2πn
N
(5.37)
Using 5.37 the coeﬃcients FDFT is found from m=0...N-1 which then will make out the spectrum.
The equation to ﬁnd these coeﬃcients is shown in equation 5.38:
FDFT(m) = 1
N
N−1
X
n=0
fd(n) · e−jxnm
(5.38)
In equation 5.38 FDFT(m) is the calculated spectrum sampled at m in the frequency domain. When
using the FFT algorithm the needed amount of data is reduced signiﬁcantly compared to the DFT.
It works by using equation 5.39:
FFFT(m) =
N−1
X
n=0
fd(n) · e−jxnm
(5.39)
Which means that the output of MATLAB needs to be manually divided by N.
An example of connection between a discrete sampled signal and its spectrum is shown in 5.9.
Figure 5.9: Example of a sampled signal and corresponding spectrum created by DFT
From ﬁgure 5.9 it can be seen that sampling from 1 to N on the signal side actually contains the
entire signal since it is periodic. This also results in a periodic spectrum, but for an analysis it is
suﬃcient to look at the baseband.
An extraction of the imported waveform is suﬃciently to do the analysis, so an array of N = 1000
values is enough for a good frequency approximation. By inspecting the timestamps of the values it
can be seen that PowerFactory is sampling at a frequency of fs = 10kHz.
12The theory of DFT and FFT is based on chapter 11 [p. 528 - 531 [34]]

Performing the FFT
The FFT is performed by using Matlab’ fft() command and the output is divided by N to obtain
the DFT. As seen on ﬁgure 5.9, several images of the spectrum appears, and since it is valid to only
look at one, the baseband is chosen. It can also be seen on ﬁgure 5.9 that the baseband is mirrored
in the y-axis, so by choosing the spectrum from m=1...N
2 + 1 and multiplying the amplitudes with
two, the other side contributions are added as well, and the spectrum is complete.
With a fs = 10kHz it results in a frequency diﬀerence between the coeﬃcients at f0 = fs
N = 10Hz
which is more coeﬃcients then needed. Therefore when displaying the results only coeﬃcients which
are a multiplication of the fundamental frequency (50Hz) are displayed. The FFT of the current
waveform and the signal itself are shown on ﬁgure 5.10:
Figure 5.10: Inverter output as simulated in PowerFactory
As expected the FFT on ﬁgure 5.10 displays only the fundamental frequency, which has an amplitude
equal to the peak value of the current waveform.
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
61
Adding harmonic distortion
Next part is to introduce harmonic distortion at the correct harmonics. In order to do this, is matter
of multiplying the corresponding harmonics with the given percentage of distortion as a percentage of
the fundamental. The used values are based on a 6 pulse inverter and with a 3% line reactor. Figure
5.11 shows a section of typical harmonic current percentages for such inverter, these values are used
as the harmonic content.
Figure 5.11: Section of Table 5.1 [p.21 [8]] including typical harmonic current values
With the harmonic content inserted, an inverse FFT is performed, so the distorted waveform can be
inspected along with the spectrum. This is done by reversing the steps from the FFT. (1) Dividing
with 2 to recreate the two side spectrum. (2) Append the spectrum image so the spectrum contains
the same amount of samples as the signal. (3) Multiply with N so that the values are in the same
format ready for Matlab iﬀt(). (4) Perform inverse FFT where MATLAB utilizes equation 5.40
fd(n) = 1
N
N−1
X
m=0
FDFT(m) · ejxnm
(5.40)
The result of the added harmonic content can be seen on ﬁgure 5.12, where it should be noted that,
for clarity reasons, the y-axis scale of the spectrum is halved. This is done to make it easier to observe
the harmonic content, the fundamental still has a amplitude of approximately 10 kA.

From ﬁgure 5.12 the harmonic content is now visible in the spectrum, and on the current waveform.
The fundamental 50 HZ waveform, is still dominant but the eﬀect of the harmonic currents is clear
in additional peaks. The highest current peak is now 12.5kA.

5.6.4
Harmonic discussion
Using active ﬁlters to decrease harmonic currents content, has the main disadvantages of the added
cost compared to passive ﬁlters, but as seen through this section, the controllability and high perfor-
mance of the active ﬁlter often makes it the preferred choice for larger installations.
As countries install more renewable generation an increase in power electronics will be implemented
as well. This will result in more harmonic source connected to the grid, hence making the matter
of harmonic distortion more important in the years to come. This is indicated by the requirements
stated in table 5.5 which are more strict than the requirements from IEEE 519-1992.14 This could
be a result of Denmark having a higher electricity generation of source behind power electronics, and
therefore a need for more strict set of rules regarding harmonic currents are implemented.
The Matlab code used for harmonic distortion calculations can be seen in appendix A.2.
5.7
Flicker
When light sources encounter voltage ﬂuctuations they emit a change in luminance. If these ﬂuctua-
tions are tiny the change in luminance is not visible to the human eye, but as the ﬂuctuation becomes
higher these changes will start to irritate the eye and in worst cases resulting in an epileptic attack.
For these reasons there are limits on ﬂicker emissions from ﬂuctuating productions facilities such as
wind, PV and BESS.
These limits are determined by the long term ﬂicker disturbance factor Plt,i which is given for each
unit (inverter in this case) and a total allowed ﬂicker contribution Glt which is a value given for the
voltage level under the same transformer station15:
Un ≤35kV →Glt = 0.5
(5.46)
Since Glt is total contribution for all units, the allowed value per inverter (16 inverters in the BESS)
Plt is determined by equation 5.47:
Plt = Glt ·
3

Si
Sk
→Plt = 0.5 ·

445.32MVA = 0.045
(5.47)
Where Sk is the short circuit power determined in equation 5.36.
With the individual and total boundary limits determined, the ﬂicker emissions for one inverter is
determined using equation 5.48:
Plt,i = ci(Ψk) · Si
Sk
(5.48)

and for an array of Ψk which is the the short circuit impedance angle in the POC.

this results in the impedance of the transformer:

5.8
Other power quality categories
This section will brieﬂy discuss the remaining power quality categories that has not been covered
yet. These categories have in common that they are not suited for simulations, or that they depend
too much on the loads of the grid to set up simulations/scenarios that will cover these categories
suﬃciently
5.8.1
DC content
According to [10] the DC component of the delivered AC current cannot exceed 0.5% of the nominal
current. To verify it would require a measurement. Ideally the inverter output wouldn’t contain any
DC component, but small diﬀerences in the SPST’s can result in a small DC component.17 If a too
high DC component is detected several passive and active methods can be utilised to reduce it. This
is beyond the scope of this report and will not be discussed further, but for additional information
we refer to [36].
It is important to ensure a low DC component, since a DC component can cause asymmetrical
magnetization in the transformer, leading to overheating and harmonic voltage emissions.
16Flicker veriﬁcation section (4.4.3) [p.37 [10]]
17Causes of DC component and mitigation methods[36]
==PAGE:85==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
67
5.8.2
Asymmetry
Asymmetry describes the diﬀerences between phase values of the 3 phase AC current. If the AC
current is perfectly symmetrical they are phase shifted 120◦between each other and their peak values
are equal. An asymmetric current could be if one phase has a higher amplitude value. According to
[10] any asymmetry cannot exceed 16 A.
Asymmetry could occur if several single phase loads are connected, but not evenly distributed between
the phases.
5.8.3
Interharmonics
Just like harmonics contains distortion at the characteristic harmonic orders, a small distortion is
transmitted at interharmonics meaning harmonic at half orders.
h =



(5.52)
Like regular harmonics a table deﬁnes the boundary units for the ﬁrst 3 interharmonics (shown in
equation 5.52) depending on the SCR value. These values are shown in table 10 [10].
The procedure is then to inspect the interharmonics table from the type test, to ensure that the
percentages is lower than the boundary limits.
Interharmonics higher than the fundamental impacts, included heating eﬀects, and for interharmonics
close to the fundamental ﬂicker through voltage deviations is a concern.18
5.8.4
Higher frequency components
The last power quality category deﬁnes the limits for currents at higher frequencies ranging from 2
- 9kHz. For a BESS this size, the consultation document [10] states that the TSO in the speciﬁc
case should determine the emission limits for currents with a frequency higher than 2kHz. Similar
approach as interharmonics should be taken, by consulting the type test of the inverter, to ensure its
compliance. If the amplitude of these high frequency components are too high, they can cause EMC
shielding problems at telecommunication equipment.
18Impacts of interharmonics [p.814 [37]]

5.9
Frequency regulation and emulated inertia
This section describes the frequency regulation control implemented in a BESS, where the focus lies
on showing how a BESS can be beneﬁcial when it comes to inertia response (0-5 seconds) and primary
frequency response (5-30 seconds).
5.9.1
Droop control and requirements
Frequency control utilises a droop controller, which operates between the two frequencies f1 and f2
(These frequencies can vary depending on requirements). For these simulations requirements of DK1
are used where, f1 and f2 are 49.80 and 50.20 Hz respecting. The frequency regulation requirements
are shown in ﬁgure 5.15:
Figure 5.15: Frequency regulation requirements [p. 49 [10]]
From f1 and f2 a droop factor can be calculated by utilizing one of the frequencies in pu. The droop
factor R is then calculated so that reaching this frequency will trigger a 100% load response:
R = 1 −0.996
1
= 0.004
(5.53)
The droop parameter along with a 0.01 Hz deadband is inserted into the frequency control. fmin =
47Hz and fmax = 52Hz on ﬁgure 5.15 are the frequencies where the BESS is allowed to disconnect
from the grid.
5.9.2
Emulated inertia
Although the consultation document does not state any requirements in relation to emulated inertia,
this project decided to include this since a tendency of increased renewable generation results in a
==PAGE:87==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
69
lack of inertia in the system.
Emulated inertia is obtained by the use of a ﬁrst order lag diﬀerentiator with a gain. Such a block is
a standard macro from PowerFactory with a transfer function shown in equation 5.54
G(s) = sK ·
1
1 + Ts →
sK
1 + Ts
(5.54)
The numerator of the transfer function contains a diﬀerentiator with a gain (sK), where the gain
represents the emulated inertia constant HSyn of the BESS. This approach is based on the equation of
motion, where the superposition principle has been applied assuming that small frequency deviations
can be neglected.
∆Pelec ≈2HSyn
d∆ω
dt
(5.55)
Where the speed (ω) can be replaced with frequency since calculation are in pu.
The last part of the emulated inertia equation (5.54) is a ﬁrst order lag (low pass ﬁlter) inserted to
improve the system frequency behaviour, removing unwanted harmonics, cutting down overshoots
and smoothing the emulated inertia contributions.[38]
An emulated inertia control path is inserted as an additional loop in the original frequency control
common model as seen in ﬁgure 5.16:
Figure 5.16: Frequency control common model with emulated inertia loop
The eﬀect of the emulated inertia is that it decreases the change of frequency. In the inertia response
this results in a improve of the ﬁrst frequency drop, due to the slower change meaning the frequency
is higher when the primary frequency control kicks in. It does not aﬀect the steady state frequency
that the grid will stabilize at after the event, that is the role of the primary frequency control (droop
controller).
For veriﬁcation of a functioning frequency control with the emulated inertia controller, the develop-
ment has been performed in the test grid on ﬁgure 5.5. This is done because in such a small grid it
is considerably easier to simulate over and under frequency scenarios, and thereby ensuring that the
frequency control is functioning as intended.
Figure 5.17 are a result of several simulations in the test grid, with a general load increase of 50
%, where the HSyn parameter has been increased by each simulations. Figure 5.17a illustrates how
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
70
(a) Emulated inertia eﬀect on frequency response
(b) Emulated inertia battery current
Figure 5.17: Eﬀect varying HSyn during a load event of PLoad + 50%
increasing HSyn parameter improves the inertia response and how the minimum frequency value be-
comes higher. Figure 5.17b shows that by increasing HSyn the battery current overshoot is being
reduced.
For the simulations an inertia constant of HSyn = 10 has been chosen. This is due to inertia response
not being part of the consultation document requirements, so the eﬀect of the inertia controller
is limited to a minimum.
At the moment Mauritius grid contains plenty of rotating machines,
which means that low inertia is not a problem. As more renewable generation is connected and
some traditional generation decommissioned, the matter of supplying more emulated inertia can be
reconsidered.
Since the BESS contains no rotating mass the physical meaning of HSyn is not applicable, and it
should be seen as a controller gain. It keeps the name nonetheless because the inﬂuence resembles
that of inertia in a synchronous machine.
5.9.3
Simulation results
Simulations are done in the Mauritius grid where the BESS is inserted at a 22 kV busbar of the power
plant of St. Louis.19
The simulated scenario is a N-1 criteria where the highest loaded unit is tripped. This is done during
an evening peak of 2013, where the total load of Mauritius is at 478.05 MW, and the St. Louis power
plant is supplying 65.3 MW with one of their nine units out of service. The remaining eight units are
combined loaded at 60.9 % with G7 being the highest loaded unit at 11.9 MW20.
It should be noted that all units in operation are equipped with droop control governors, which are
operating with a droop factor of R = 0.045 meaning a frequency deviation of ±2.25 Hz would require
a full response.

busbar. This results in a lower current through the transformer (TXCB_1) connecting the 66 kV
and 22 kV busbar and thus a lower voltage drop. Transformer and loads causing this behaviour can

5.10
Voltage support
Maintaining voltage at a system can be rather complicated due to the fact that reactive power can’t
be transmitted over long distances. This translates to voltage support being done by several smaller
devices (usually shunt capacitors and inductors) dispersed through the system. Due to characteristics
of transmission lines, varying loads results in variations in the reactive demand of the system. The
need for voltage regulation at remote location, along with the needed ﬂexibility due to load variation
are requirements that a BESS can fulﬁl.
5.10.1
Regulation principles
According to the consultation document a BESS (rated at 1.5 - 24 MW) used in the dynamic simu-
lations should be able to operate with three diﬀerent reactive power regulation principles.
• Q - Regulation
Regulation of reactive power independent of the voltage and active power. A constant reactive
power set point (export/import) and control active power from that point. An example of this
type of regulation is illustrated as the horizontal line on ﬁgure 5.19.
• PF - Regulation
Power Factor (PF) regulation is taking the active power (import/export) into account as well.
It does this by regulating reactive power from a constant PF (cosφ), meaning that reactive
power is regulated proportional to active power, and thus keeping PF constant. An example of
PF regulation is shown as the inclining line on ﬁgure 5.19.
• Voltage - Regulation
Voltage regulations is using the droop principle, where the measured parameter is the voltage
and the regulated variable is the reactive power. An under voltage result in a lagging PF and
reactive power is delivered, whereas an over voltage would result in a leading PF and reactive
power being imported. An example of voltage regulation using droop is shown on ﬁgure 5.20.
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
73
Figure 5.19: Q and PF regulation illustrated on a capability curve [p.53-54 [10]]
The PQ controller from the template is equipped with slow PI controller for set point tracing, which
performs proportional voltage support. This means that in order to obtain the desired reactive power
regulation functionality, the model has to be modiﬁed.
• Q regulation is obtained by setting iqmin and iqmax to the desired reactive power import/export.
• PF regulation is implemented by removing the voltage input at the PQ-controller and replacing
it with a PF measurement. This changes the iq control path to regulate with PF. The Vref
signal needs to be replaced with a PF parameter, which includes the desired PF value.
• Voltage regulation requires a new common model containing a voltage droop controller, that gets
the busbar voltage as input. The output ∆Vref would then be connected to the PQ-controllers
Vref signal.
==PAGE:92==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
74
The choice of regulation principle can depend on what the BESS should be used for or the stiﬀness of
the connection. For BESS implementation inside a power plant where the BESS would be the least
inﬂuential unit, PF or even Q regulation is an obvious choice. Whereas a BESS located remotely
from any conventional generation units could be beneﬁcial with voltage regulation.
Figure 5.20: Voltage regulation on reactive power requirements plot [p.55 & 59 [10]]
Since the main purpose of this section is to describe the voltage support capabilities of a BESS, the
voltage regulation is chosen for further simulations.
Voltage droop controller
Implementation of voltage regulation is done by inserting a new common model that contains a
droop controller. Input is the voltage measurement Vin where it relies on a steady state calculation
to provide the reference voltage V0. The deadband is set to dbV = 0.005 which corresponds to a
maximum allowed deviation voltage of 0.5%. In the PQ common model the proportional voltage
support has been removed and replaced by a PI controller for set point tracing, where the ﬁlter and
PI controller parameters are similar to the corresponding component on the idref loop21.
The droop factor itself is determined from the maximum allowed voltage deviations, and Qmax, Qmin
21Common model diagrams of modiﬁed BESS composite frame, voltage droop controller and modiﬁed PQ common
model can be seen in Appendix A.8.2 ﬁgure A.9, A.10 and A.11.
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
75
which would be delivered by the TSO. In this case the voltage ratings from ﬁgure 5.20 are used along
with the programmable PF limits of the inverter (0.6 inductive and 0.6 capacitive)[9]:
R = 1 −0.9
0.6
= 0.167
(5.57)
An ∆iq error signal is sent as feedback from the charge controller to the PQ controller where it is
added to the control signal after the ﬁlter.
The structure and functionality of the voltage droop controller resembles that of the frequency con-
troller but with one diﬀerence. The frequency controller calculates the ∆P signal around an oﬀset of
POff = 0, where the voltage droop controller calculates around an oﬀset of Voff = 1 since the wanted
voltage is 1 pu, and deviations from this should be handled.
5.10.2
Simulation results
For voltage support simulations on the Mauritius grid, an over voltage is created by decreasing the
taps on the Step Up transformer (SUT). It is assumed that the tap changer on the SUT is an on
load tap changer (OLTC), which is a reasonable assumptions with the increasing automations in the
power sector. OLTC controls the voltage at the 22 kV busbar from a voltage measurement which it
compares with a reference value. The scenario is a loss of this measurement, resulting in the OLTC
decreasing taps to increase the voltage. It takes the OLTC 10 seconds to change a tap and it manages
to decrease the tap 4 times, before the fault is detected and the OLTC is forced into manual control,
where it is being brought back to its original tap.
As expected the voltage is increasing in a "stair" shaped curve, where the maximum voltage without
the BESS reaches Vmax = 23.33 kV and with the BESS that over voltage is diminished to Vmax = 23.08
kV22
Figure 5.21 shows the BESS output during the tap event, where it can be seen that both the voltage
droop controller and the frequency controller are being activated. Under frequency occurs due to the
higher busbar voltage and connected loads operated at a constant PF (0.96 inductive). This means
that as the voltage increases so does the apparent power in order to comply with the PF, which means
more active power and a frequency drop.
The reactive power response to the increased voltage is to increase import of reactive power. From
iqref_out on ﬁgure 5.21 it can be seen that it ﬂattens out before the next tap event, this is due to the
voltage actually being stabilized at 1 pu, which does not ﬁt with the 22 kV busbar voltage. This is
because the voltage droop controller get its measurements from the "LV BESS" busbar, which actually
is regulated down to 1 pu. iqref_out also shows three increases instead of four, this is because at the
fourth tap change, the BESS shortly after reaches its reactive power limits, where it is kept until the
voltage starts to drop.
2222 kV busbar line to line voltage and frequency is displayed in Appendix A.10.3 ﬁgure A.23
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
76
Figure 5.21: BESS output during tap event
From ﬁgure 5.21 it can be concluded that the BESS is able to detect over/under voltages situations,
and apply an appropriate reactive power response. When the fault is being cleared it manages to fall
back to its original power set points
5.11
Capacity ﬁrming
Capacity ﬁrming is unlike voltage and frequency support meant as a preventive functionality. Where
voltage and frequency support are acting on ﬂuctuations on the grid, capacity ﬁrming focus on
smoothing power outputs from renewable generation that could result in grid instability.
For illustrating how capacity ﬁrming works, photovoltaic generation will be added to the test grid,
where it will be connected at the 0.415 kV busbar in parallel with the BESS. A PowerFactory template
"PhotovoltaicSystem_0.4kV_0.5MVA" will be used to simulate the PV.
This template contains some control functionality that is unwanted, since the purpose is to smooth
the PV output by the use of the BESS. This means that 1. Voltage support, 2. Active power
reduction and 3. Set point tracing in active power loop functionality are disabled. Further
==PAGE:95==
Chapter 5. Dynamic Behaviour
77
changes include assuming a steady state DC voltage, removing temperature and solar radiation ramps
and replacing them with measurement ﬁles instead. Which implements generation depending on
measurement ﬁles, instead of users enabling a ramp function to simulate a radiation/temperature
change. Since the main focus of this project is BESS, the changes and implementation of PV is
described in appendix A.8.4.
5.11.1
Capacity ﬁrming model
The capacity ﬁrming approach used is the smoothed characteristic maximum power curve (SCMP),
which relies on measurements to create a maximum PV curve Pm(t).
This curve is then being
smoothed for removing any ﬂuctuations Pscmp(t). By the use of a factor m the given daily optimal
power reference is found Popr(t) = m · Pscmp(t), in this way the controller can take seasonal variations
into account.
Capacity ﬁrming is then obtained by evaluating the diﬀerence between the measured production
Ppv(t) and the optimal production Popr(t):
Pc(t) = Ppv(t) −Popr(t)
(5.58)
From equation 5.58 a capacity ﬁrming reference Pcf(t) is calculated by evaluating the derivative of
Pc(t):
Pcf(t) =







Pc(t)
for
Ll < dPc(t)
dt
< Ul
Ul∆t + Pc(t −∆t) for
dPc(t)
dt
> Ul
Ll∆t + Pc(t −∆t) for
dPc(t)
dt
< Ll
(5.59)
Where Ul represents the upper limit and Ll the lower limit, which are parameters for the capacity
ﬁrming input. The output to BESS is the diﬀerence between Pcf(t) and Pc(t):
D(t) = Pc(t) −Pcf(t)
(5.60)
The capacity ﬁrming principles illustrated above is based on the PV capacity ﬁrming section from
[39]. Additional coding include insertion of a minimum time cap Tcf for capacity ﬁrming, to avoid
unnecessary stressing of the battery. An ampliﬁcation factor Amp is multiplied to the output D(t).
This is done since the PV system is rated at 0.5 MW, which means that capacity ﬁrming compensation
from the 5.2 MW rated BESS would not ﬁt the PV ﬂuctuations if not scaled down.
Implementation is done by creating a composite frame, that contains three common models.
• Measurement ﬁle including the Popr(t)
• P measurement from the PV output Ppv(t)
• Capacity ﬁrming calculation using the DigSILENT programming language DPL
The composite frame including the 3 common models (shown in ﬁgure 5.22) is inserted in the BESS
composite frame, where the output is used as an additional ∆Pcf_ref signal in the PQ-controller. The
needed modiﬁcation for including capacity ﬁrming along with a brief introduction to the PV model
is shown in appendix A.8.423.
23Understanding of the DPL code is obtained by studying the code supplemented with section 3 from [40]
==PAGE:96==

Figure 5.22: Capacity ﬁrming composite frame

added along with a single irradiance increase, this new ﬁle is then used as input to the PV model.

of PV ﬂuctuations is reduced.
==PAGE:98==
6
Generation scheduling
When BESS and renewable power sources are implemented in the power grid, the needs for a smart
scheduling system increases. A power scheduling system, also called a PMS, is the main tool in order
to maximise the use of BESS and renewable power sources. A scheduling system that does not control
the BESS itself, but controls the power set point for each generating unit and the BESS in a speciﬁed
power plant or -grid. Based on the set point from the PMS it is then the BESS control system that
control the batteries and inverters, in order to meet the set point from the PMS. By combining PMS,
BESS and renewable power sources, the power production costs for a power plant or -grid decreases
due to the characteristics of nearly zero marginal production cost for renewable power sources and
the ability of a BESS together with a PMS to increase the power plant power production eﬃciency.
As stated in the former chapters a BESS can help with stability issues in the millisecond and second
domain. Furthermore, it is in this chapter investigated if the BESS, on top of stability contribution,
can lower the power production costs of a power plant or a whole power grid with the help of a PMS.
Hence making the BESS an economical feasible investment.
Such a generation scheduling model or PMS that minimises the cost of power production and take
the diﬀerent parameters into account, can be based on a unit commitment problem. The unit com-
mitment problem is an optimisation problem with the objective of minimising the power production
costs. The layout of an unit commitment problem can diﬀer from system to system depending on
the detail level of the problem deﬁnition and the speciﬁc system to be controlled. Based on the
diﬀerent systems and aims, the number of constraints can range from few to many. The size of the
unit commitment problem can, as stated, range from a few generators to a entire power grid with
several voltage levels and types of power plants.
In this chapter, two diﬀerent unit commitment problems are made. The ﬁrst model is for a single
diesel power plant with multiple generators, while the second unit commitment model is implemented
in order to minimise production costs for an entire grid.
Though the unit commitment models established in this chapter, are primarily a proposal to a dy-
namic PMS model, they can also be used to calculate the economy of a BESS integration in a power
plant or power grid. This takes advantage of the unit commitment problem characteristics of min-
imising the costs. Therefore to be able to see the inﬂuence of a BESS from an economical perspective,
the simulations made with the established models are performed with and without BESS. In this way
it is possible to see the decrease of power generation costs with and without diﬀerent sizes of BESS,
and compare the saving with the capital expenditure (CAPEX) of the BESS.
In the following sections, the two unit commitment models deﬁned here are established. Afterwards
these two models are used to determine the economical feasibility of a BESS implementation.
80
==PAGE:99==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
81
6.1
Power Plant unit commitment
The unit commitment problem for a power plant describes a more basic unit commitment problem,
compared with the power grid unit commitment problem described later in this chapter. In this
model it is assumed that the set points for active- and reactive power are given at the POC of each
power plant for a deﬁned time period, e.g. 24 hours. A time period of 24 hours is seen as a good
compromise between solving time and suitable results. A longer time period will result in longer
optimisation problem solving time, while a smaller time period will result in a worse optimisation.
Based on these set points, the generation from each generating unit in the power plant is scheduled
in order to minimise the total power production costs.
A Power plant can consist of a given number of e.g. diesel motors attached to generators, BESS
and/or renewable generation. A power plant with diesel engines combined with renewables and/or
BESS, is generally known as a hybrid power plant.
Losses in equipment and lines between the generating units and the POC are in this model neglected
due to the small distances between generation point and POC. If the power losses for a given power
plant is relevant to add, these can be added to the model without problems as a function of power
production. In order to investigate the economy for a BESS implementation, these losses are not
relevant.
In general an optimisation problem is based on three parts. An objective to be minimised or max-
imised, a given number of constraints to be kept and variables that the optimisation problem needs
to determine. Therefore in the following sections, these three parts are deﬁned and described. After-
wards, it is described how these three parts combined is solved.
6.1.1
Objective
For this project the objective of the unit commitment problem is to minimise the power production
costs for a period of 24 hours. Basically if a power plant consisting of PV, some older low eﬃciency
diesel engines and some high eﬃciency new diesel engines, the usage priority has the order of PV,
new diesel engines and old diesel engines based on the marginal power production cost.
The total cost of the power generation in this project is obtained from the sum of several types of
cost:
• Fuel costs - Cfuel[e]
• Operation costs - Cop[ e
hon]
• Start up costs for conventional power generating units - Cstart[ e
Start]
• Emission costs - Cem[ e
MWh]
In many island power grids, emission costs are not implemented. Though this is implemented in the
unit commitment problem in this project. This is due to the aim of also minimising emissions as well
as power production costs. Depending on the size of the emission cost, this cost can in some cases
inﬂuence the optimisation in a smaller or larger degree. By changing this cost in the later performed
==PAGE:100==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
82
simulations, the inﬂuence of the emission cost on the economical feasibility can be seen.
The diﬀerent costs in the objective function are each multiplied with their speciﬁc variable. The
variables multiplied with the costs are in an optimization model called decision variables. When these
decision variables are multiplied with the costs, the objective function in equation 6.1 is obtained:
min : z =
T
X
t=1
N
X
n=1
Cfuel(n, t) + Onc(n, t)Cop(n) + Cem(n) + S+(n, t)Cstart(n)
(6.1)
The diﬀerent decision variables are described later in this chapter.
In the objective function t is the hour and n is the the number of each speciﬁc conventional gener-
ating unit, while the decision variables Pprod(n, t), Pon(n, t) and S+(n, t) are respectively the power
production, the unit on status and the start binary value in hour t, as it also can be seen stated in
the following sections. As it can be seen in the objective function, the sum for each hour and every
generating unit is considered. In this way the total cost for the whole speciﬁed period is minimised.
The fuel cost Cfuel(n, t) for each unit and at each hour is calculated as
Cfuel(n, t) = HeatRate[ MJ
MWh] · Eu,c(n, t)[MWh]

i
(6.2)
where Eu,c(n, t) is the energy used by the diesel engine and generator to produce the electrical power,
and is deﬁned later in this chapter. The emission cost Cem is calculated as
Cem(n, t) = HeatRate[ MJ

he

i
(6.3)
The use of a BESS and/or the power production from renewable power sources is in this project not
assigned a cost. It is assumed that BESS and renewable power sources does have maintenance costs,
but these are not inﬂuenced by the amount of energy produced and/or charged and discharged. The
zero cost associated to the BESS and renewable power sources in the objective function, secures that
the system utilise as much power from the renewable power sources as possible.
On equation 6.1 it can be seen that the decision variables are ordered in a matrix, where each row
speciﬁes each unit, while each column speciﬁes each hour. This is done in order to reduce and simplify
the optimization model.
6.1.2
Constraints
The constraints in the unit commitment problem speciﬁes both limits and parameters for batteries,
diesel engines and demand as well as limits for the decision variables. Some constraints apply for
every hour and for every unit, while others are limited to the ﬁrst hour or to a certain unit type. In
the following subsections these constraints are described and established.
Battery constraints
When batteries are implemented in the unit commitment problem, it implies an extended number of
constraints in order to describe the limitations and parameters of the batteries and their corresponding
decision variables. Decision variables that are speciﬁcally for batteries are:
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
83
• SOC(i, t): State of Charge [MWh]
• Pb,−(i, t): Value of discharged active energy in time step t [MWh]
• Pb,+(i, t): Value of charged active energy in time step t [MWh]
• Pb,bin(i, t): Binary value that states if the battery is charged or discharged in time step t (1 if
the battery is charged and 0 if the battery is discharged. If the batteries are not charged or
discharged this value can be both 1 or 0, but in this case the value is indiﬀerent)
• Qb(i, t): Value of reactive energy in time step t [MVArh]
The ﬁrst battery constraint states that the SOC in the ﬁrst time step of the optimization, is the state
of charge of the battery in the last time step of the last optimization. This value is stated as SOC0.
SOC(i, 1) = SOC0(i)
∀i ∧t = 1
(6.4)
If the constraint in equation 6.4 is disregarded, the optimisation problem would set the SOC to the
maximum value in the ﬁrst time step. Because of the idea that the generation scheduling problem is
ran continuously through many periods, the battery is not necessarily full at this time step. Therefore
the battery SOC cannot be assumed to be at the maximum level.
Through the later performed simulations, it was seen that the best result where obtained if the SOC
was constrained to 50 % at the end of the optimization period. Therefore equation 6.5 is added to
the problem.
SOC(i, end) = 50
∀i ∧t = 24
(6.5)
As earlier stated in the battery theory in chapter 2, a battery with a speciﬁc energy capacity has a
minimum SOC that is greater than zero in order to prolong the battery lifetime. Furthermore an
upper limit below the maximum capacity of the battery is also set. This is due to the capacity in
a modern Li-ion battery, is best kept if the SOC is kept within a certain range, that both have an
upper- and lower limit. Especially in a stress period, e.g. when the BESS is used to secure stability,
this stress causes an increased degradation if the battery SOC is in the high or low range. Apart from
helping to decrease power generation costs and increasing the use of renewable generation, the BESS
should also be able to contribute to secure grid stability if needed. Therefore there should always
be a energy buﬀer in the battery that can help with e.g. frequency regulation without causing the
battery degradation to increase unnecessarily much. The energy buﬀer is set to 10 % in the upper-
and lower limit.
These limits for the SOC are stated in the constraints in equation 6.6.
(100 −DODmax,%(i) + 10) ≤SOC(i, t) ≤90
∀i ∧t
(6.6)
The SOC is changing value every hour depending on the value of the charging and discharging. The
constraint stating this is shown in equation 6.7.
SOC(i, t) = SOC(i, t −1) + Pb,+(i, t)
Eb,max(i)100 −Pb,−(i, t)
Eb,max(i)100
∀i ∧t
(6.7)
==PAGE:102==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
84
In equation 6.8 and 6.9, limits for the decision variables stating the value of charge and discharge
each hour can be seen. In these constraints the binary decision variable Pb,bin is used to state if the
battery is charged or discharged. Pb,bin is 1 if the battery is charged and 0 if it is discharged. This
ensures that the battery is not charged and discharged at the same time.
0 ≤Pb,+(i, t) ≤CRate(i)Eb,max(i, t)Pb,bin(i, t)
∀i ∧t
(6.8)
0 ≤Pb,−(i, t) ≤CRate(i)Eb,max(i, t)(1 −Pb,bin(i, t))
∀i ∧t
(6.9)
The battery in a BESS is the component that enables the possibility of the active power charging
and discharging. Furthermore it is possible for a BESS to deliver inductive- and capacitive reactive
power to the connection bus. This is made possible by the inverter(s) in the BESS as described in
chapter 2. Though each inverter has some active- and reactive power limits. These limits is handled
by implementing a maximum magnitude of apparent power of each inverter.
|Pb,−(i, t) + Pb,+(i, t) + jQb(i, t)| ≤Sb,max(i)
∀i ∧t
(6.10)
Depending on the inverter characteristics, the relationship between active- and reactive power, de-
scribed with the power factor, is limited to a certain range. An illustration of this operation area can
be seen on ﬁgure 6.1.
Figure 6.1: PQ capability of inverter
Figure 6.1 illustrates the range of apparent power that the inverter can deliverd. These limits are
implemented in the optimisation model with the constraint seen in equation 6.11.
|Pb,−(i, t) + Pb,+(i, t) + jQb(i, t)| ≤|Pb,−(i, t) + Pb,+(i, t)|
pfb,max(i)
∀i ∧t
(6.11)
Conventional generation unit constraints
Constraints specifying the diesel generation units are power production limits, diesel engine energy
usage, minimum up- and down time and deﬁnition of on/oﬀhours, start and stop. In order to specify
these constraints, following decision variables are introduced:
• Pc(n, t): Produced active energy by unit n in time step t [MWh]
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
85
• Eu,c(n, t): Used energy by unit n in time step t [MWh]
• Plow,c(n, t): Value of the energy production below the minimum power production value in the
maximum eﬃciency range by unit n in time step t [MWh]
• Sc,+(n, t): 1 if unit n is started in time step t (binary)
• Sc,−(n, t): 1 if unit n is stopped in time step t (binary)
• Onc(n, t): 1 if unit n is producing in time step t (binary)
• Qc(n, t): Value of reactive energy by unit n in time step t [MVArh]
• Pr,cur(r, t): Curtailed renewable energy by unit r in time step t [MWh]
In other unit commitment problems with coal- or biomass power plants, ramp up and ramp down
times are implemented as well. These are not taken into consideration in this optimisation problem,
due to the fast reacting capabilities of diesel engines. Though if the optimisation is performed in
short time steps of e.g. every minute, ramp up- and ramp down times should also be implemented
depending of the diesel engine characteristics. During on hours for the diesel engine, the diesel engine
and generator both have a minimum and maximum production limit. Furthermore the diesel engine
have a certain power production range where it produces power with its maximum eﬃciency. As seen
on ﬁgure 6.2, the eﬃciency for a certain diesel engine decreases from around 80% to its minimum
limit at around 40%.
Figure 6.2: Example of diesel engine eﬃciency as a function of diesel engine load (BWSC Data)
In order to implement this eﬃciency behaviour, the decision variable Plow,c is used to state the value
of the power production below the minimum power production value with maximum eﬃciency. This
decision variable can be seen implemented in equation 6.12, which states the minimum and maximum
active power production of the diesel engine and generator.
Pc,%,min,HR(n)Onc(n, t)
100
Pc,max −Plow,c(n, t) ≤Pc(n, t) ≤Onc(n, t)Pc,max
∀n ∧t
(6.12)
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
86
The eﬃciency has an inﬂuence on the energy usage of the diesel engine to produce the required
kinetic energy in the generator.
The energy usage is calculated as the power production output
divided with the eﬃciency. The eﬃciency and energy usage cannot directly be calculated and used
as decision variables, because this makes the optimization problem nonlinear. As later described
in the project, a nonlinear problem combined with binary decision variables, increases the solving
time signiﬁcantly. This workaround with implementing Plow,c is therefore introduced. Because the
objective is to minimise the cost, it is not necessary to calculate the accurate cost in the objective
function. It is only necessary to state a penalty, which penalties power production below Pc,%,min,HR.
In this way the optimization only determines a low power production of each unit when strictly
necessary.
Therefore instead of calculating the eﬃciency and divide this into Pc(n, t), in order to ﬁnd the used
energy, Pu,c(n, t) can be found as seen in equation 6.13.
Pu,c(n, t) = Pc(n, t)
ηc,max(c) + Plow,c(n, t)Penalty
∀n ∧t
(6.13)
From equations 6.12 and 6.13 it can be concluded that if a speciﬁc diesel engine unit is allowed to
produce power at a lower eﬃciency, a low eﬃciency penalty is causing the energy usage per electric
power output to increase. Furthermore the energy usage increases, the lower the electric power output
is. This secures that the optimisation model keeps each unit power production at a high eﬃciency
level, when it is economically feasible.
The penalty is in this project set to 0.5, but based on simulations, small changes in this value does
not eﬀect the obtained solution. It is however important that the value of the penalty is not too high,
which can cause a solution with higher cost than necessary. Higher costs than necessary can be if the
penalty is so high, that a new unit is started for e.g. only one hour just to prevent a generating unit
to produce power at a lower eﬃciency, which can result in a not optimal solution.
The decision variable Plow,c is limited to the range seen in equation 6.14.
0 ≤Plow,c(n, t) ≤Pc,%,min,HR −Pc,%,min
100
Pc,maxOnc(n, t)
∀n ∧t
(6.14)
Apart from having a limit on the active power production, each unit does also have a limit on the
reactive power production, stated by the maximum allowed power factor. This limit can be seen in
equation 6.15.
|Pc(n, t) + jQc(n, t)| ≤
Pc(n, t)
pfc,max(n)
∀n ∧t
(6.15)
In the objective function from equation 6.1, a cost per engine start is added to the cost. Therefore a
constraint stating an engine start is added to the problem, by using the Onc decision variable.
Onc(n, t) −Onc(n, t −1) ≤Sc,+(n, t)
∀n ∧t
(6.16)
Both the decision variable Sc,+ as well as Sc,−are necessary for establish the minimum up and down
time constraints. The constraint that deﬁnes the engine stop decision variable Sc,−can be seen in
equation 6.17
Onc(n, t −1) −Onc(n, t) ≤Sc,−(n, t)
∀n ∧t
(6.17)
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
87
When a start or stop of a unit has occurred, the unit can have some limits stating that is has to stay
in that state for a speciﬁed minimum time. This means that, depending on the engine, an engine
cannot stop at hour t and start again in hour t+1. These minimum up and down times are stated in
equation 6.18 and 6.19.
Onc(n, t + 1 : MinUpTime(n)) ≥Sc,+(n, t)
∀n ∧t
(6.18)
Onc(n, t + 1 : MinDownTime(n)) ≤1 −Sc,−(n, t)
∀n ∧t
(6.19)

The demand constraints in the power plant unit commitment problem deﬁnes that the active- and
reactive power demands for every hour needs to be met. Therefore the sum of active- and reactive
power output from each diesel, battery and PV unit needs to be equal to the demand every hour.

If it is assumed that the batteries themselves have negligible losses, the losses in the BESS described

In the study seen in [42], a round trip eﬃciency at 92.63% is given. This eﬃciency is slightly higher

is split in two in equation 6.20, the round trip eﬃciency is also split in two, and stated as ηb(i, %).
The round trip eﬃciency is 92.63% which gives ηb(i, %) equal to 96.24%.

Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
88
Security constraint
In power grids several measures are taken in order to secure stability in the grid and be able to
cope with a power loss on the production side. Some of these measures are spinning reserves and
N-1 criteria. When renewable energy is implemented together with a power plant, the security of
supply decreases. For example if it is assumed that a PV plant produces 30 MW this production can
suddenly be decreased in larger degree by a cloud blocking the sun. If the synchronized diesel engines
in this hybrid power plants are running at near maximum load, a sudden drop in solar power cannot
quickly be replaced with power from the diesel engines, causing a drop in power delivery at the POC,
and thereby, a frequency drop in the grid. In small island systems, such a drop in power production
from a larger PV plant can cause larger frequency drops. Therefore if the security of the scheduled
supply from a given power plant is desired, a security of supply constraint can be added to the unit
commitment model.
In this project, such a constraint states that the free synchronized power and the power capabilities
of a BESS at all times, shall be larger than or equal to the actual power production from the PV
plant. This constraint can be seen in equation 6.23.
R
X
r=1
Pr(r, t)−
R
X
r=1
Pr,cur(r, t) ≤Onc(n, t)
N
X
n=1
Pc,max −Pc(n, t)+CRate(i)Eb,max(i, t)ηb,%(i)
100
∀n ∧t
(6.23)
With a maximum allowed DOD of e.g. 80 %, the SOC is limited to 30% - 90% in the generation
scheduling model. Therefore, the BESS always has energy to help with stability issues. The amount
of support in a shorter timeframe is therefore only limited to the battery size and c-rate.
6.1.3
Implementation in optimization tool
The optimization problem established in this section, is a mixed integer linear problem (MILP).
As stated, solutions have been implemented in order to withstand from a nonlinear mixed integer
problem , and by doing that, decreasing the computing time.
To solve this problem the GUROBI optimization problem solver is used together with the Yalmip
Matlab package. The Yalmip Matlab package sorts the stated constraints and decision variable in
the right format, so they can be implemented in the GUROBI solver.
One of the aims with the power scheduling problems deﬁned and programmed in this project, is
to make it as general as possible. In this way the program made in Matlab is made independent
of the power plant size, -type and parameters. This is done by importing data from Excel ﬁles and
automatically establishing the size of matrices and variables necessary to solve the problem. The data
is in the program sorted in two diﬀerent types: generation data and demand data. The Matlab code
for import and establishing of these data in correct formats, can be seen in appendix A.3.1 Demand
Data Import and Generation Data Import. These data are then read in the power scheduling program
itself. The Matlab code for the power scheduling program can be seen in appendix A.3.1 Main Script.
The optimization can, in a real life application, be ran every hour for 24 hours in advance. In this way
the program is continuously updated with the newest values for demand and solar power forecasts.
At the start of each optimization, the ﬁrst hour value of each decision variable needs to be set to
==PAGE:107==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
89
the current state for the speciﬁc variable and unit. A ﬂow diagram illustrating this continuously ran
algorithm can be seen in ﬁgure 6.3.
Figure 6.3: Power plant power scheduling ﬂow chart
6.1.4
Simulations & Results
As earlier stated, the established unit commitment problem can be used to investigate the economical
feasibility of a BESS implementation. In the simulations made in this section, an implementation of
a BESS in a given power plant, is investigated by running the optimization program for every day in
a given year. The revenue obtained by implementing the BESS is obtained as the diﬀerence between
the power production costs with and without the BESS. This diﬀerence is used to calculate the basic
economical values such as Net Present Value (NPV) and Internal Rate of Return (IRR) .
The NPV is basically an economical value that shows what a given investment is worth at the time
of investing. This value is calculated with the net cash ﬂow at time t, CF(t), the investment cost,
==PAGE:108==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
90
CF0, and the decided discount rate1, rdisc, as it can be seen in equation 6.24.
NPV = −CF0 +
T
X
t=1
CF(t)
(1 + rdisc)t
(6.24)
The NPV for an investment that is economically feasible is greater than zero, and the larger NPV
the better is the investment seen from an economical perspective.
The IRR shows the annual return rate of an investment. Hence the IRR shall be larger than the
determined discount rate, if the investment is economically feasible. The IRR value is obtained by
setting the NPV to zero in equation 6.24, and solve for IRR.
0 = −CF0 +
T
X
t=1
CFt
(1 + IRR)t
(6.25)
Power Plant and data Description
Due to the plan of using the Mauritius grid and thereby also a Mauritian power plant, as the case, the
BWSC build St. Louis power plant was chosen for these simulations. Though data from this power
plant has, due to political reasons, been impossible to obtain. Therefore the model uses data from
the BWSC build and operated power plant in Rabai Kenya. This selection is based on the fact that
Rabai use the same type of Wärtsilä 18V46E engines as the St. Louis power plant. The Rabai power
plant is placed approximately 20 km north west of Mombasa Kenya and operates primarily as a base
load power plant running with a power factor close to unity. It consist of ﬁve Wärtsilä 18V46E diesel
engines producing combined maximum 90 MW. Based on the power demand data from the BWSC
O&M department in the period from January to April 2017 and the power demand data for 2016, a
power demand matrix is established. This is done by using the 2017 data as reference and based on
the diﬀerence between each month in 2016, extrapolating the 2017 data to the whole year of 2017.
This demand matrix can be seen in appendix A.4, where the data is given in average MW per half
hour. Because of the fact that the Rabai power plant is running with a power factor close to unity,
no reactive power demand needs to be met and hence the reactive power constraint in equation 6.22
is neglected.
Some of the diesel engine and generator data used in the unit commitment optimization problem,
can be seen in table 6.1. The rest of the data can be seen in appendix A.5.

of the ﬁve units.

Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
91
Because of the time period of half hours, the speciﬁc type of diesel engines has no limits regarding
ramp up time as well as minimum on and oﬀhours. The constraints stated in equation 6.18 and 6.19
are therefore also neglected for this particular case.
The battery data used in the simulations only speciﬁes C-rate, half-trip eﬃciency ηb(i, %) and
maximum DOD. As described in section 2.8.1 a high energy NMC is the preferred choice of battery
in these type of application. Because the energy has the highest priority compared with power capa-
bilities and, in order to lower the battery degradation, the C-rate is set to 0.8. The maximum DOD
is set to 80 %, meaning that the battery SOC in this power scheduling system will not come below
30% due to the earlier described desired stability support capabilities.
In order to have PV curves for the approximated location of the Rabai power plant, half hour
solar radiation data for Mombasa for the whole year of 2005 is used. These data are obtained from
SoDa2. The PV power data is approximated in such a way, that the maximum radiation value corre-
sponds to active power value of 1 pu. In the diﬀerent simulations, depending on the set PV size, the
pu active power value is mulitplied with the speciﬁc PV size of the respective simulation.
Simulation descriptions
In order to see the inﬂuence of changes in BESS size and price, renewable integration and fuel price,
several simulations with diﬀerent parameters have been performed. The values for the simulations
with a BESS size larger than zero is then compared with the values obtained from the simulations
with no BESS implemented, but with same size of renewable integration. By doing this, it is not the
economical feasibility of renewable integration that is investigated, but only the economical aspect of
the BESS implementation inside power plants with diﬀerent values of renewable integration.
The base cases are therefore the Rabai power plant with diﬀerent sizes of PV installed without
BESS integration.
The generation scheduling in these base cases are still using the established
power scheduling optimization model. This in order to make a fair comparison between the diﬀerent
simulations.
As explained earlier in this chapter, the fuel costs are calculated based on an approximated value
for the thermal energy used by the diesel engine to produce the electrical energy. Therefore a new
Matlab program is made, which takes each units active energy values for each time step of each day,
and calculates the actual fuel costs. The Matlab code for this can be seen in appendix A.3.1 Fuel Use
Calculation Matlab function.
Simulations are as stated ran for diﬀerent PV and battery sizes. These diﬀerent simulations and the
results are described in the following sections.

In this simulation series the maximum PV power output is set to 30 MW. All simulation are ran until
the battery energy capacity drops below 80% of the initial energy capacity. Hereafter, the CAPEX
