Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
93
is a low case projection of a emission cost in the US. It should be noted that in several of the countries
where BWSC has build diesel engines power plants, no such thing as emission cost is implemented.
Though such a cost as the one used in this project, gives an approximated value of pollution to the
environment. Smaller changes in the emission cost does not aﬀect the results in larger degree. If the
emission cost is neglected, the IRR becomes -1% for the case with a BESS size of 5 MWh.
In order to see the inﬂuence of the security constraint seen in equation 6.23, a simulation for the case
with a PV size of 30 MW and a BESS size of 5 MWh without the security constraint is performed.
In this case the NPV and IRR becomes -2.51 mio. e and -8.10 % respectively. The decrease in these
economical values shows how much of the gain of a BESS implementation is due to the ability of the
BESS to act as an "stand by" spinning reserve. A lot of extra cost is added when the diesel engines
needs to be ramped down, or a new diesel engine needs to be started, in order to be able to cope with
lost PV. This extra cost can be decreased if a BESS is added to the system.
The results in table 6.2 shows that a BESS implementation in a hybrid power plant with a PV
size of 30 MW is not economically feasible, no matter the size of the BESS. There are however several
factors that can increase the feasibility of a BESS implementation. If the case with a BESS size of 5
MWh in a system with security constraint and without emission cost is used, it can be observed that:
• The cost of the BESS has to decrease to 0.75 [ e
MWh] before such a BESS implementation is
economically feasible.
• The fuel cost has to reach a fuel cost larger than or equal to 410 [e
T ] in order to make a BESS
implementation economically feasible.
• The cost/gain of stability help is not taken into account in these cases, and if the price of such
a gain is implemented, the feasibility increases.
The economical break even cost of BESS and fuel are not unrealistic values. For the cost of fuel this
assumption is based on fuel prices in the past, the fact that fuel in the future will be used less causing
an increase in price, and the present fuel price war between diﬀerent countries. In a meeting held
between BWSC and ABB, the assumption from ABB where that the cost of BESS will decrease to
approximately 0.8 mio. e per MWh in the near future.
In ﬁgure 6.4 an illustration of how the NPV is with diﬀerent fuel- and BESS costs can be seen.
==PAGE:112==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
94
Figure 6.4: NPV as a function of Fuel- and BESS price for case with PV = 30 MW and BESS = 5
MWh and with emission cost
From ﬁgure 6.4 it can be seen how a procent wise decrease in BESS price has a larger inﬂuence on
the NPV compared with a procent wise increase of the fuel price. As earlier stated it can also be
seen that only a small decrease in BESS price and a small increase in fuel price can make this exact
case economically feasible.
The third point that can increase the feasibility of a BESS implementation is an inclusion of the
gain in stability help. In Chapter 5 it is shown how a BESS can help to increase the stability of
a grid. In many of the islands systems where BWSC operates, the stability of the grid is of high
importance. Therefore increased stability is a gain that also needs to be taken into account when
deciding if a BESS implementation is feasible or not.
As seen in table 6.2, an implementation of the cost of pollution increases the feasibility of BESS
implementation. Especially in the case with PV size of 30 MW and with a BESS size of 5 MWh, the
diﬀerent cost does not have to change much before the implementation becomes economically feasible.
From the values in table 6.2 it can also be seen how an increased BESS size from 5 MWh and
upwards, decreases the economical feasibility. In the approximated battery lifetime row, it can be
seen how the lifetime increases for increasing BESS sizes. This indicates that the use of the BESS
decreases with larger BESS sizes. Larger CAPEX and less use of the BESS causes the economical
feasibility to decrease for larger BESS sizes in the case with a PV size of 30 MW.
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
95
An view of how the values seen in table 6.2 has been obtained, can be seen ﬁgure 6.5, which shows
the used calculation sheet.
Figure 6.5: NPV and IRR calculation sheet for case with PV = 30 MW and BESS = 5 MWh
From the calculation sheet the diﬀerent cost with and without BESS for every year can be seen.
The power production cost for the system with BESS increases every year, due to the approximated
degradation of the batteries.
The total cost each year for the system with BESS is then subtracted from the yearly cost for the
system without BESS, in order to ﬁnd the net cash ﬂow each year. Based on the calculated cash ﬂow
and discount rate, the NPV and IRR are found by using respectively equation 6.24 and 6.25.
When the scenario with and without the BESS is compared, as in ﬁgure 6.5, it can be seen how
the total active power production increases when a BESS in implemented. This is due to the losses in
the BESS during charging and discharging. The demand and PV power is the same in both scenarios,
==PAGE:114==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
96
hence the power delivery at the POC must be the same. When a BESS is used, losses occurs, which
needs to be supplied by the diesel engines. The gain of implementing a BESS can be seen in the fuel
use row for the scenario with BESS. This fuel consumption is smaller than in the scenario without
a BESS, because the BESS with the proper generation scheduling model can help to increase the
eﬃciency of the diesel engines.
From simulations in this simulation series, examples of how power production costs is decreased
by the use of a BESS, can be seen. An example of how the optimised use of a BESS, decreases the
power production costs, can be seen in ﬁgure 6.6.
Figure 6.6: Example of how a BESS can decrease # of running diesel engines in case with PV = 30
MW and BESS = 10 MWh
Figure 6.6 shows an accumulated power production matrix. This means that the ﬁrst curve, orange,
is the power output for diesel engine 1, the second curve, yellow, is the sum of the power output from
diesel engine 1 and 2, the third curve, purple, is the sum of the power output for diesel engine 1, 2
and 3, and so on. The blue dotted line represent the demand each half hour. This means that if the
cumulated power production is above the demand curve, the BESS is charged, and if the accumulated
power production is below the demand curve, the BESS is discharged.
At the start of the day, the demand is at the maximum level, meaning that all ﬁve diesel engines are
running at full load. Though at the end of the day, the demand has decreased, but is not low enough
==PAGE:115==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
97
that four diesel engines can supply the demand themselves. Therefore without a BESS a start of the
ﬁfth diesel engine would be necessary. In this case with a BESS implemented, the BESS is charged
before this time period, and hence it can be discharged and thereby replace the ﬁfth diesel engine.
Furthermore the four running diesel engines can run with maximum eﬃciency instead of running ﬁve
diesel engines with a lower eﬃciency.
Power production costs increases when the security constraint is implemented. Figure 6.7 and 6.8
shows an illustration of the impact of the security constraint implementation and why the power
production costs increases.
Figure 6.7: Example of how security constraint inﬂuence the operation in case with PV = 30 MW
and BESS = 10 MWh
Figure 6.7 shows the same curves representing the accumulated matrix, but for a diﬀerent day. In
this case the security constraint is implemented, causing the necessity to run four diesel engines in
the PV production hours, and some of them with a low eﬃciency. Comparing ﬁgure 6.7 with ﬁgure
6.8, which shows the same day but for a model without the security constraint, the reason for the
increased cost can be seen.
For the scenario in ﬁgure 6.8, the fourth diesel engine is oﬀduring most part of the PV production
hours, which results in only three diesel engines running at maximum eﬃciency.
==PAGE:116==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
98
Figure 6.8: Operation same day as in 6.7, but without security constraint in case with PV = 30 MW
and BESS = 10 MWh
Simulations with other PV sizes
Apart from simulations with the Rabai power plant added a 30 MW PV farm, the same simulations
have also been made for other sizes of PV combined with diﬀerent BESS sizes.
The economical
values for each of the other simulations where very low, and hence not economical feasible. Though
the second best result, based on NPV and IRR is with a PV size of 15 MW and a BESS size of
5 MWh. The NPV and IRR for this scenario is respectively -1.20 e and -0.48 %. The economical
values for all cases apart from this, are much lower than the cases with a PV size of 30 MW.
Conclusion on simulations & results
The simulations in this chapter are made with a base load power plant. This means that the power
output is less ﬂuctuating that e.g. a peak load power plant. A BESS implementation is very usable
for power plants with high variations in power output. This is because with higher variations, the
more the BESS can be used at both the peaks and lows, and thereby decreasing the fuel costs. By
performing these kind of simulations on a base load power plant, the simulations are performed on
the worst case scenario.
This needs to be taken into consideration when, these worst case results are used and concluded upon.
Apart from this fact, it can be seen on the calculated break points, that the prices for fuel and BESS
does not have to change much in order to make the results economical feasible.
==PAGE:117==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
99
Furthermore an implementation of BESS decreases the vulnerability of fuel prices, as it can be seen
on the decrease in fuel use with a BESS implementation, making the investment more secure. It
is diﬃcult to predict how the fuel price will develop in the coming decades. To be less vulnerable
against the ﬂuctuations in fuel prices, it is therefore an extra beneﬁt with a BESS implementation.
6.2
Security constrained unit commitment
The unit commitment problem for the power plant can, with some extra constraints, be extended so
it is able to make a generation scheduling for a whole grid. These extra constraints are implemented
in order to secure that the power ﬂows in the grid does not exceed the capabilities of the lines.
An unit commitment problem with power ﬂow constraints is often referred to as a Security Con-
strained Unit Commitment problem (SCUC). Apart from securing that no lines are overloaded, the
characteristics of the optimization model, ensures that the optimal power ﬂow together with the gen-
eration is obtained.
In order to make the best possible optimization, AC load ﬂow should be used.
In this way the
voltage limits are also taken into consideration. The idea behind the grid generation scheduling is
that each power plant is, by the grid scheduling problem, seen as a single unit, which is given an
active- and reactive power set point for a time period, e.g. the following 24 hours. Hence the grid
scheduling problem is equivalent to the power plant scheduling problem coupled with extra power
ﬂow constraints. This hierarchy is illustrated in ﬁgure 6.9.
Figure 6.9: Optimization model hierarchy
By adding AC power ﬂow constraints, the optimization becomes nonlinear. The non-linearity coupled
together with the binary decision variables seen in the power plant generation scheduling problem,
makes such an optimization a mixed integer nonlinear problem. Such an unit commitment problem
with AC power ﬂow constraints is very diﬃcult to solve, resulting in a too long solving time, if the
model should be ran continuously. Therefore some measures needs to be made, in order to decrease
the solving time, but still have the reactive power contribution of AC power ﬂow constraints.
==PAGE:118==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
100
6.2.1
Power ﬂow constraints
An unit commitment problem without security constraints covering a whole grid, can end up with an
optimal solution which exceeds the power transport capabilities of the transmission lines. Furthermore
losses in transmission lines are not taken into account, if power ﬂow analysis are not implemented
as the security constrain. This causes a supply that is less than the demand plus losses. Another
parameter that is not taken into consideration in a basic unit commitment problem without AC power
ﬂow constraints, is the voltage at each busbar, hence limits can be exceed.
It is therefore necessary to implement power ﬂow analysis in an unit commitment when the problem is
covering a larger grid. AC power ﬂow analysis cannot be implemented together with the power plant
unit commitment. In order to calculate the active power losses in the grid, as well as approximated
maximum active power ﬂow, a DC power ﬂow analysis mismatch function is added as a constraint to
the already described power plant unit commitment problem.
In DC power ﬂow equations, only the active power is considered. The active power balance for each
bus in the system is given by equation 6.26.
Pi,in −Pi,out = |Vi|
J

|Vj|(Gij)cos(θij) + Bijsin(θij)
(6.26)
In this DC power ﬂow analysis the following parameters are assumed:
• All voltage magnitudes are equal to 1
• Small voltage angle diﬀerences
• G«B: G is therefore approximated equal to zero
If these three assumptions are implemented in equation 6.26, it can be reduced as seen in equation
6.27.
Pi,in −Pi,out =
J
X
j=1
Bijθij
(6.27)
In order to get this power balance implemented as a constraint in a system where power system values
are given as vectors, equation 6.27 is converted to matrix form.
Pi,in −Pi,out = Bθ
(6.28)
Here is B a N x N matrix where N is the number of buses in the system, while θ is a n x 1 vector
containing each bus voltage angle. The B matrix contains the susceptances of each line. Hence the
line shunt capacitance, line resistance and transformer ratios are not taken into consideration when
the B matrix for the power grid is established. An example of a B matrix for a four bus system,
where all buses are connected, can be seen below.
B =

In the B matrix example, index L represents pure inductive susceptance.
When the DC power ﬂow equation is implemented, the unit commitment problem is therefore ex-
tended with one additional decision variable θ(i, t) and one additional constraint based on equation
6.28.
Pc(i, t) + Pb,−(i, t) −Pb,+(i, t) + Pr(i, t) −Pr,cur(i, t) −DP(i, t) = Bθ(i, t)
∀i ∧t
(6.30)
This constraint seen in equation 6.30 replaces the constraints seen in equation 6.20 and 6.22. Fur-
thermore it can be seen that the length of the vectors for all the power generation units and demand
have changed from the number of the speciﬁc unit and demand to the number of their bus placement
in the system. This is done to be able to implement the DC power ﬂow matrix equation together
with the generation and demand in a single constraint.
The right hand side of equation 6.28 deﬁnes the power ﬂow in each transmission line. Therefore this
can be used to set up a constraint deﬁning the maximum power ﬂow in each transmission line.
−Pij,max ≤Bθ(i, t) ≤Pij,max
∀i ∧t
(6.31)
The last constraint in the SCUC problem with DC power ﬂow constraints speciﬁes the voltage angle
limits. The limits are set to be ± 15 degrees, or ± 0.2618 radians.
−0.2618 ≤θ(i, t) ≤0.2618
∀i ∧t
(6.32)
The unit commitment model added the DC power ﬂow constraints, results in a security constrained
unit commitment problem with DC power ﬂow constraints. This problem is still a linear optimisation
problem, and can therefore be solved within an acceptable time, in this case under half a minute.
Therefore this security constraint unit commitment problem is solved as the ﬁrst part of two opti-
misation problems in the generation scheduling program. The aim with this ﬁrst optimization is to
determine the binary decision variables, which are then used in the second optimization problem.
In the second optimisation problem, more precise AC power ﬂow constraints are implemented. The
binary decision variables are decided in the ﬁrst optimisation problem, and hence become constants.
Therefore the second optimisation problem is a nonlinear optimization problem, which makes it faster
and easier to solve compared to a mixed integer nonlinear optimization problem.
Because AC power ﬂow is introduced, reactive power is also taken into consideration. This is done
by use of the apparent power. The apparent power balance vector can be derived from the formula
for apparent power seen in equation 6.33.
S = V I∗= V

(6.33)

In the optimization model, the apparent power balance equation is implemented as two constraints,

(6.39)
In nearly every power grid there are grid codes which states the amount of spinning reserve, vacant
synchronised capacity among others. In Mauritius grid codes it is stated that N-1 criterion needs to
be met and that there at all times should be minimum 10% of spinning reserve [p.9 [44]]. Spinning
reserve deﬁnes the amount of excess power that should be possible to get from the online power
plants.
In order to meet these two demands, the constraint in equation 6.40 and 6.41 are deﬁned.

The last constraint added to the second optimization problem, is implemented in order to secure
the current ﬂowing in each transmission line is within each of the speciﬁc transmission line limit.
Where the SCUC problem with DC power ﬂow constraints stated a maximum power ﬂow, it is
in this nonlinear optimisation problem possible to add the maximum current instead. Basing the
transmission line limits at maximum current instead of maximum power, is a more accurate measure.
This is because power ﬂow changes with voltage. Higher voltage allows for higher power ﬂow, causing
the power limits to be inaccurate. Whereas the current is the parameter that in reality is focused
when transmission line limits are considered. The current is given as the product of the admittance
and voltage. Converted to matrices and vectors, the current is therefore the magnitude of the product
==PAGE:121==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
103
of the Ybus and the bus voltage vector, as seen in equation 6.42. Due to the pu values, this calculated
current needs to be less than or equal to the maximum pu current of each line.
|Ybus(i, j)(V (i, t) −V (j, t))| ≤Imax,pu(ij)
∀i ∧∀j ∧t
(6.42)
The constraints belonging to the AC power ﬂow analysis replaces the constraints in equation 6.30
and 6.31 in the SCUC problem.
6.2.2
Implementation in optimization tool
As earlier stated, the aim is to have a model that automatically imports data from a list e.g. an
Excel ﬁle, and automatically generate the optimisation problem and achieve a solution. Apart from
importing the values from the generating units and the demand, it is in this SCUC problem also needed
to import the transmission line data. Furthermore, a program has been made which establishes the
B and Ybus matrices based on Excel ﬁle data. The main part of the program can be seen in ﬁgure
6.10. The whole program can be found in appendix A.3.2 Grid Data Import.
Figure 6.10: Loop in Matlab code that automaticly generates Ybus and B matrix
The part of the program in the B and Ybus generation Matlab ﬁle, which can be seen in ﬁgure 6.10,
is the part where the B and Ybus matrices are established. The input is the pu value of each line and
transformer, as well as each line and transformers to- and from busbar number. In the import ﬁle
where all the grid data is set, each busbar is given a speciﬁc number, which is used to determine and
set the to- and from busbars. In the for loop, the diagonal of the B and Ybus matrices are the total
sum of all impedances for Ybus and susceptances for B, which goes to and from the speciﬁc bus. When
the column and row written to in the for loop is not the diagonal, the speciﬁc value is the impedance
==PAGE:122==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
104
or susceptance between the two busses in row r and column c.
In ﬁgure 6.10 it can also be seen that the Ybus matrix consist of resistances, inductances, transformer
pi-model and shunt capacitances, while the B matrix only consists of the lines inductive susceptances.
This design in Matlab has a high versatility and allows for solving diﬀerent systems independent
of grid size, number of generating unit, transformers among others.
When the import has been performed, the ﬁrst unit commitment with DC power ﬂow constraints is
performed, where after the unit commitment with AC power ﬂow constraints is performed. A ﬂow
chart for this code can be seen in ﬁgure 6.11.
Figure 6.11: Grid power scheduling ﬂow chart
6.2.3
Solver
With regards to the SCUC problem containing DC power ﬂow constraints, the GUROBI solver is
used together with the earlier described Yalmip Matlab extension. This secures a low solving time of
the MILP. For the SCUC NLP using AC power ﬂow constraints, a solver capable of solving nonlinear
problems is used. The used solver is the Ipopt solver. Several solvers such as Baron and Knitro can
solve these problems faster than the Ipopt solver, but is not used because of the cost of these.
6.2.4
Simulations & Results
In order to perform correct simulations and obtain results that can be used to estimate real life
application, following parameters needs to be known:
==PAGE:123==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
105
• Power plant data - maximum MVA and MW capacities, minimum load, heat rate, start up cost
among others (as used in the hybrid power plant simulations).
• Demand values in a deﬁned time frame, e.g. hourly intervals from each bus in the system.
• Production curves from the renewable energy sources.
• Data for transmission lines, transformers and capacitor banks.
• Production history data for each power plant - Used to be able to compare obtained results
with real life data.
As earlier stated a PowerFactory ﬁle of the Mauritius power system has been obtained. From this
ﬁle data of lines, transformers, capacitor banks and generator sizes is extracted. It has however not
been possible to get periodic data from CEB over demand and production, as well as the remaining
power plant data.
Like for the hybrid power plant unit commitment problem, and the following simulations, this grid
SCUC model can be used to estimate results for the Mauritius grid, such as economical beneﬁts with
implementation of a BESS model. Though because of the limited data on the power plants, apart
from the St. Louis power plant, such a model which gives usable approximated economical values
can therefore not be established.
Because of the obtained Power Factory ﬁle of the Mauritius grid, simulations are still made for
the 66 kV grid, with the power line data from the Power Factory ﬁle. The grid itself is described
in section 1.3.
Small hydro power plants and wind turbines are neglected because of their lim-
ited and weather dependent power production. Therefore, the simulated grid consist of nine power
plants, three diesel engine power plants, one gas turbine power plant and ﬁve biomass/coal power
plants. Data for the power plants used in the simulations can be seen in appendix A.3.3. Data for the
MVA and MW capabilities is partly obtained from the PowerFactory and from educated assumptions.
The aim of the following simulations is to estimate and show how the developed model can be
used to determine the best economical BESS location. In order to show this with the Mauritius 66
kV grid as the practical case, the rest of the power plant data does not need to be correct in order
to show the BESS capabilities and determine the best location for the BESS. Though the data needs
to be set up in such a way that it is ensured that the power from the IPPs is used ﬁrst, secondly
the newest diesel engine power plant and thereafter the rest of the diesel engine power plants. If the
power production from the IPPs and CEB owned diesel engine power plants, as well as the renewable
power is not enough to supply the demand, the gas turbine power plant is used. As long as the
production cost of the diﬀerent power plant is sorted in this speciﬁc way, the result for the best BESS
location will be the same, no matter the value of the power production costs for each power plant.
Apart from transmission line data and generator sizes, a peak demand for a given day can also be
obtained from the PowerFactory ﬁle. Based on these peak values for the load on each busbar in
the system and a daily nominal load curve proﬁle from the Mauritius 2013-2022 strategy plan [p.32
[14]], a daily load proﬁle for every load in the system is established. Based on the estimated peak
day demand, transmission line, capacitor bank and power plant data in the PowerFactory ﬁle, the
==PAGE:124==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
106
simpliﬁed network is established. Data for this network can be seen in appendix A.6.
Apart from working as a continuous ran optimisation program or calculating the economical feasibil-
ity of a BESS implementation, the SCUC model can also be used to estimate the best geographical
placement of a BESS system seen from diﬀerent perspectives, such as economical, power loss and min-
imum voltage diﬀerence. Simulations where these three perspectives are investigated, are described
in the next sections.
Placement of BESS - Minimise power production cost
Firstly a simulations is ran for the speciﬁc day without a BESS implemented. Thereafter simulations
are ran for a 10 MWh BESS added to each busbar. Based on the value of the objective, the econom-
ically best bus to implement a BESS system can be decided. The objective value of each simulation
can be seen in table 6.3.

As it can be seen in table 6.3 there is an economical beneﬁt in adding a BESS, no matter which busbar
the BESS is implemented. The largest economical gain is obtained if the BESS is implemented at
busbar number 26. Busbar number 26 is the FUEL SE busbar, where one of the IPP is connected.
From this busbar only a single line goes to the rest of the grid.
The reasons for the decrease in power production costs is the same as described in the hybrid power
plant generation scheduling simulations. The optimisation model tries to supply the demand with
the highest power production eﬃciency, least possible amount of synchronised power plants and least
number of starts as possible. The BESS is thus used to minimise the overall costs with a active- and
reactive contribution to the grid, that can be seen in ﬁgure 6.12.
==PAGE:125==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
107
Figure 6.12: Hourly charging, discharging and reactive power from BESS
In this optimisation problem with AC power ﬂow calculations, a comparison between the values
on busbar 26, with and without BESS, cannot be made. This is because the optimisation model
can end up with another power production result when a BESS is implemented compared to the
scenario without a BESS. This means that the reactive power output from the BESS, not necessarily
contributes to a higher or lower voltage at busbar 26. Though the reactive power contribution from
the BESS, can in some cases free some capacity at the other power plants, and by doing that increase
the total power production eﬃciency. With regards to the active power contribution, a look at the
numbers in table 6.4 shows that the BESS makes it possible to have less power plant running hours,
which decreases the operation- and fuel costs.

The IPPs can be prioritised by setting their fuel costs lower than the other power plants. Though the
IPPs in these simulations are given a higher priority, as it can be seen on the example in ﬁgure 6.13,
the IPPs are producing less power than CEBs own power plants in the periods where CEBs power
plants are active.
Figure 6.13: Comparison of active power production in percentage of own capacity between an IPP
and a CEB owned power plant
As seen in ﬁgure 6.13 the FUEL IPP is producing each hour. When the demand increases, CEB
owned power plant Fort Victoria is started and runs at its minimum power production with maxi-
mum eﬃciency. Because of the lower set fuel cost of the IPP, power production from this power plant
is decreased in order to make it possible for Fort Victoria to produce power with its highest eﬃciency.
If this scenario is unwanted, and the IPPs should produce power at maximum eﬃciency instead, this
can be changed by increasing the penalty in equation 6.13 for the IPPs.
During each of the simulations, where the objective values is listed in table 6.3, the total active
power loss for the given day is also calculated. The total active power loss can be seen in table 6.5.

Table 6.5: Power losses for without BESS and with BESS added to each busbar in the 66 kV network.
From table 6.5 it can be seen that the BESS placement that yields the lowest objective is not the
placement that gives the lowest power loss in the system. This emphasizes the importance of the
broad picture. It is not enough for an optimisation model to have the aim of minimising power losses.
It can be that a power plant can produce power at a lower cost, even if the extra active power losses,
that maybe come with producing power from the power plant with lower power production costs, is
taken into consideration.
Placement of BESS - Minimising voltage diﬀerence
Throughout the grid there are voltage diﬀerences. The aim in the grid is to have a voltage at all
busbars as close to 1.00 pu as possible. A low voltage causes increasing power loss, while high voltage
can damage equipment. Though sometimes it is economically beneﬁcial to have a voltage at a busbar
at the lowest allowed value, which was the case in the last simulations where the aim was to minimise
the power production costs. In this simulation there where several hours with busbar voltage levels
of 0.95 pu. It is important to notice that the voltage on these busbars, is not the highest reachable
voltage at the speciﬁc time. If the voltage limits in the SCUC model are narrowed closer to 1.00 pu,
e.g to be between 0.98 and 1.02 pu, these voltage limits can be respected.
The aim of the SCUC model is to minimise the power production costs. In this simulation, the lowest
power production cost is obtained when some of the busbar voltages reaches 0.95 pu. If the voltage
limits are narrowed closer to 1.00 pu, the power production costs would increase.
If the voltage limits between 0.95 and 1.05 pu are the applicable limits, no busbar in the Mauritius
grid have problems with too low voltage levels.
Though this can be a case if the voltage limits
were narrower or the demand increases. Because the described SCUC model runs an AC power ﬂow
analysis, it is possible to extract the voltage in pu from each busbar.
Therefore, to show how the developed SCUC model can be used to determine the best location for a
BESS in order to minimise the largest voltage diﬀerence between 1 pu and the lowest voltage value,
the objective of the model is changed from minimising the power production costs, to minimising the
objective seen in equation 6.43.
Minimise : z = max(|Vref −Vi(t)|)
∀i ∧t
(6.43)
To analyse the grid with the developed SCUC model and the new objective, simulations are ﬁrst ran
without a BESS implementation. In table 6.6 the lowest voltage value on each bus can be seen.

If a 10 MWh BESS is coupled to busbar 7, which has the lowest voltage, and new simulations are ran,
the lowest voltage becomes 0.9901 pu. If a BESS is added to any other busbar in the grid, the lowest
voltage becomes 0.9861 pu. Therefore it can be seen that in order to decrease voltage diﬀerences,
busbar 7 is the best busbar to place a BESS unit. Busbar number 7 is the Sottise substation. This
substation has only one connection to the rest of the grid, with a relatively high reactance, which also
can be seen in grid data in appendix A.6. This causes the substation to be one of the weakest voltage
wise, and a BESS implementation beneﬁts therefore the system with voltage support at the Sottise
substation. It is important to notice that with regard to voltage support, capacitor banks can help
with this too. Though they do not provide controllability and cannot help with frequency support.
6.3
Implementation of generation scheduling model
The grid scheduling model or PMS developed in this chapter, is a simpliﬁed version of what could be
a real PMS system in the Mauritius grid. Apart from the optimisation problem, the inputs to this
model has high priority. The closer the values of the forecasts for renewable energy and demand are
to the actual values, the better the solution obtained becomes.
As seen in ﬁgure 6.9, the idea described in this project is that the grid- and power plant scheduling
models are run in two diﬀerent levels. The grid scheduling model determines the output to the grid
from each power plant, based on forecasts for demand and renewable energy sources connected to the
grid. In order to clarify this further, ﬁgure 6.9 can be extended as seen in ﬁgure 6.14.
==PAGE:129==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
111
Figure 6.14: Power Scheduling structure between PMS and lower level optimization models
Figure 6.14 has arrows showing two way communication. In order to make an usable optimisation, the
grid scheduling model needs to now the exact parameters of each power plant, microgrids, renewable
power plants and loads. These exchanged values can be:
For microgrid:
• Needed power at each time interval (From microgrid)
• Allowable change in power delivery and cost for less or more power delivered (From microgrid)
• Cost of more or less power (To microgrid)
• Changes in delivered power (To microgrid)
For "smart" Power plant:
• Power capabilities in each time interval.
Can change if e.g.
a engine is taken out due to
maintenance (From power plant)
==PAGE:130==
Chapter 6. Generation scheduling
112
• Allowed changes in power delivery without costs and allowed changes in power delivery with a
cost (From power plant)
• Cost of more or less power (To power plant)
• Changes in delivered power (To power plant)
For non controllable load:
• Demand forecast (From load)
For non controllable renewable energy source:
• Power production forecast (From renewable energy source)
The communication between the lower level and the grid optimization model can be with common
communication standards such as IEC61850. By splitting the diﬀerent systems up in levels, and by us-
ing common communication standards, systems from diﬀerent manufacturers can be used throughout
the whole grid and hence, make the implementation of smarter control easier and less proprietary.
==PAGE:131==
7
Conclusion
Throughout this project the topic of implementing BESS and PMS has been investigated, with the
goal of being able to answer the questions stated in the problem deﬁnition:
How can a battery storage solution be sized and deployed in order to maximise the use of renew-
able energy while increasing the eﬃciency of conventional power plants in Mauritius. Furthermore,
what is needed of power system control strategy and optimisation models to make the battery both
environmentally and economically feasible for Mauritius and BWSC?
7.1
Results and perspective
In this project, it has been shown how a PMS with optimisation models can increase the feasibility of
the BESS, with regards to both the economy and environment. Seen from the perspective of BWSC,
it is shown how an optimisation model together with a BESS can be a future economical feasible ad-
dition to new, as well as older, hybrid power plants. By extending the power plant generation model
to a whole power grid, it has also been shown how the model coupled with a BESS can decrease the
power production costs in a whole grid.
Though during the process it became clear that controlling BESS through a PMS requires general
knowledge about BESS and how it behaves in diﬀerent scenarios.
This is due to various TSO’s
tightening requirements needed to get a permit to implement BESS. Even though decreased power
production costs is the aim for many BESS implementations, the grid stability has the highest prior-
ity. Therefore, to understand the behaviour of a BESS, investigations of the main components were
performed.
The choice of battery used in this project fell on the Li-ion battery, given its high energy density, cycle
life and eﬃciency. This battery type is also experiencing a decrease in price, due to the increasing
demand from the electrical vehicle industry. By the use of ABB ESI-I 325 kW inverters, a 5.2 MW
BESS unit was put through several simulations to investigate and prove that a BESS can meet the
demands given by the consultation document from the Danish TSO [10]. This document was chosen
since Energinet.dk is ready with BESS requirements for obtaining a connection permit. It is however
an assumption that meeting these requirements would be suﬃcient to obtain a connection permit in
Mauritius.
The ﬁrst requirement is short circuit contribution.
Short circuit calculations indicated that not
only did the BESS comply, but it was also able to contribute with overload current during a short
circuit on the grid. This is important since high fault current is needed for protection equipment to
detect and clear the fault. It becomes even more important when the grid contains a lot of renewable
generation, since these sources are known for decreasing the overall fault current level.
113
==PAGE:132==
Chapter 7. Conclusion
114
Second requirement is to limit the harmonic content in the power delivered to the grid. Like fault
current, it is important because more renewable generation is delivering power through power elec-
tronics. Too high harmonic content can result in various problems such as malfunctioning protection
equipment. The ESI-I inverter is delivered with an active ﬁlter, which can decrease the harmonic
content through injection of an anti-phase current. Simulations showed how this current decreased
the total harmonic distortion, and how requirements can be fulﬁlled by tuning the ﬁlter.
Through the use of a PowerFactory model, simulations were performed showing how a BESS can
regulate active and reactive power to comply with required regulation demands. With this regu-
lation, it was proven that BESS can cooperate with traditional units in maintaining grid stability,
crucial to ensure safe operations. Besides fulﬁlling the requirements it was shown how a BESS can
further enhance grid stability by the use of emulated inertia and capacity ﬁrming. The emulated
inertia response decreases the frequency ﬂuctuation during a fault, and capacity ﬁrming decreases
the impact of ﬂuctuations from renewable generation.
With the knowledge of a BESS having the capabilities to assist in maintaining grid stability and
complying with given requirements, the task of implementing a PMS commenced.
A PMS using optimisation algorithms can be used together with a BESS to decrease renewable cur-
tailing and increase the power production eﬃciency.
In chapter 6, the developed power plant optimisation model is used to calculate the economical fea-
sibility of a BESS and from there, determine the optimal energy size of the BESS, which yields the
best economical feasibility in a hybrid power plant and a power grid.
For simulations performed for the hybrid power plant, the highest economical feasibility where ob-
tained when a ﬁve diesel engine power plant where coupled with a 30 MW PV plant and a 5 MWh
BESS. The economical results gave a NPV of -0.75 mio. e and an IRR of 0.97 % with a discount rate
of 4 %. Though these results suggest a bad investments, it should be noted that the simulations was
done for a base load power plant, which is seen as the worst case scenario. Base load power plants
are supplying power near their maximum capacity most of the time, and thereby supply power with
maximum eﬃciency. This results in less possibilities for the BESS to improve the power production
eﬃciency in a base load power plant.
By considering changes in the BESS and fuel prices, the NPV as a function of the BESS and fuel
prices was illustrated. From this illustration, it can be concluded that relatively small changes in
these two prices result in high improvements of the economical feasibility of a BESS implementation.
The BESS price is still relatively high, while the present fuel price is low seen from a historical per-
spective. It is therefore not unrealistic to assume that these prices will change in such a way, that
the economical feasibility of a BESS implementation will increase.
By adding DC- and AC power ﬂow constraints to the power plant optimisation model, a grid opti-
misation model is established. This model takes line losses and voltage drops into account. Because
of limited data from CEB, real results for economical feasibility of a BESS implementation in the
Mauritius grid, could not be obtained. Though with the established model, the best BESS placement
in the Mauritius grid is determined, both with regards to minimising power production costs and to
decrease the system voltage diﬀerences.
==PAGE:133==
Chapter 7. Conclusion
115
When optimising by reducing power production costs, the best location was found to be at the FUEL
SE busbar which suggest that the BESS is supporting the existing power plant at this busbar.
Simulations where the objective function is altered to decrease the system voltage diﬀerences, results
in a best BESS location at the Sottise busbar.
With regards to the results for the economical feasibility in this project, the economical beneﬁt
in stability contribution is not considered. Hence no revenues is obtained by contributing to the sta-
bility. If the grid is experiencing stability issues, the savings of avoiding e.g. black outs, over/under
voltage trip of grid bound components could be added as an economical beneﬁt. How to price such
events are a diﬃcult matter and could be a part of the further work.
7.2
Further Work
This section discuss work that can be done to further enlighten the subject of BESS and PMS
implementation. Areas that have not been investigated in this report, but could be interesting to
investigate, are also stated here.
7.2.1
Frequency response and abnormal operating conditions
While chapter 5 shows regulation results for both frequency and voltage control, it could be interesting
to investigate and implement the autonomous control of frequency response and fault ride through.
Frequency response is a matter of implementing two ramps, which would start when the frequency
exceeds the regulation band shown on ﬁgure 5.15. These ramps would ensure that when the frequency
returns to normal, the active power follows a slow ramp towards its pre-fault set point.
Low Voltage Fault Ride Through (LVFRT) introduces requirements during voltage drops. Depending
on the magnitude of the voltage drop and time, a requirement plot is created. As longs as the voltage
drop is within the requirements, the BESS is required to stay connected to the grid. If a voltage drop
exceeds the requirements, disconnection from the grid is allowed.
7.2.2
Modal analysis
By the use of a modal analysis, an optimal location for the BESS could be found for improving small
signal stability, which is the power systems ability to maintain synchronism, when subjected to a small
disturbance. A location could be found by identifying the oscillatory modes (complex eigenvalues)
and insert the BESS, to see if these modes improves. For increasing the small signal stability, the
eigenvalues real component should become more negative.
A modal analysis is a linear approach to the non linear power system, which means that such analysis
can only be seen as a snapshot of the system. Therefore several analysis should be performed before
making any conclusions.
The modal analysis was actually attempted on the Mauritius grid, but to complete the analysis it
was necessary to disable governor, AVR and PSS of each synchronous machine. This is a signiﬁcant
simpliﬁcation, since especially excitation systems (AVR) inﬂuence the results.
This leads to the
conclusion, that in order to perform the modal analysis, the governor, AVR and PSS should be
==PAGE:134==
Chapter 7. Conclusion
116
modiﬁed, so that the analysis can be run before any conclusion is made. The theory however is
interesting, which is why the modal analysis is mentioned in this sections. It could be interesting to
see if a BESS can improve small signal stability, and if an optimal location can be found from this
analysis.
7.2.3
LCA
A Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) of a BESS implementation could be very interesting to add to the
project. Results throughout this project indicate that a BESS decreases the emissions. Though a
question could be: "Does the BESS reduce power producing emissions enough to make up for the
emissions from the BESS production?". A LCA could answer this question by taking emissions from
manufacturing, construction and losses from the BESS into account. This is interesting because an
article mentions that for household owners in Australia, battery storage implementation is not a good
investment, if the goal is to help the environment1. Based on such a LCA, an emission comparison
between BESS and other energy storage systems can also be made.
7.2.4
Energy storage type and parameters
In this project, some abilities and applications which the BESS can perform, have been investigated.
This gives BWSC an idea of what can be provided to the costumers. Furthermore, it has been shown
how the developed models can be used to estimate the correct BESS size with regards to energy
capabilities. Though in order to be able to make a proposal to costumers, several other parameters
should be taken into account:
• Power vs. energy capabilities
• Diﬀerent combinations of cathode material if Li-ion battery is chosen
• Energy storage type: Depending on the application e.g. a supercapacitor, ﬂywheel or a combi-
nation can be suitable solution.
Further work can therefore be to extend the model, so not only the best energy size, but the best
power, energy and storage type/combination is decided. To determine this, data is needed from the
costumer before a solution can be found, where after a tender can be made to the diﬀerent energy
storage manufacturers.
7.2.5
Peak shaving and generation unit replacement
A BESS application that has not been touched in this project is peak shaving and generation unit
replacement. In Mauritius as well as other island grids, conventional units such as gas turbines, are
standing at stand by the most of the time, and are only active in some yearly demand peak hours.
Investigating if replacing a single peak load unit with a BESS unit can be an economically feasible
investment, could therefore be an area for further investigations.
1Does battery storage help or hurt the environment?
https://www.solarquotes.com.au/blog/does-battery-storage-help-or-hurt-the-environment/
==PAGE:135==
Chapter 7. Conclusion
117
With regards to peak shaving, it could also for this application be interesting to investigate the
economically feasibility of the diﬀerent kind of peak shaving, both with regards to a BESS and
renewable combination, and with regard to BESS installation in a substation where the demand
increases.
7.2.6
Real life implementation of PMS model
In the project thoughts of how to implement a real life PMS have brieﬂy been discussed. Tests in a
minor practical system could be very interesting to perform. After the basic PMS has been established
in this project, the next step is to convert the Matlab code to a format that can be used by e.g. a
CPU. Furthermore, additional work has to be done with regards to the continuous optimization and
automatically import of data. In this area agreements have to be made with weather data institutes
or companies.
When all the practical work such as CPU and communication implementation has been made, the
program can be tested for a small case ﬁrst, for example a mine. Hereafter the scale of the systems
can increase.
The developed model should in general be able to cope with power systems where there are no
electricity market. If this is the case, this can be an extension to the model, where the diﬀerent
market clearing values are incorporated in the model.

A.1
Impedance equivalents
This section includes the impedance calculations of the synchronous machine and the lines:
A.1.1
Synchronous machine impedance
The corrected sub transient impedance of an synchronous machine is deﬁned as:
ZGK = KG · (RG + jX
′′
d )
(A.1)
Where the correction factor KG is determined as:
KG = Un
UrG
·
cmax
1 + x
′′
d · √1 −cos2ϕrG
(A.2)
Rewriting equation A.1 and A.2 with information from manufacturer data sheets. UrG and cosϕrG
is given by the nameplate of the machine and RG and x
′′
d is listed in "IEC Short circuit" parameter
pane of the given type in PowerFactory. To ﬁnd X
′′
d the base value of the machine is needed ZrG =
11kv2
37.4MV A = 3.23 Using ZrG the impedance is found:
ZGK =
10kV
11kV ·
1.1
1 + 0.252 ·
√
1 −0.82

· (0.00678 + j0.252) · 3.23
Which gives an absolute value of:
ZGK = (0.0191 + j0.7082) Ω
(A.3)
A.1.2
Lines impedance
The positive sequence impedance of the lines can be read from the basic data pane in PowerFactory. It
would require geometric calculations with materials constants and length. Each line has an impedance
of:
ZL = (0.320 + j0.103) = 0.336 Ω
(A.4)
Usually manufactures would provide the impedance per meter.

A.3
Power Scheduling
A.3.1
Power Plant Unit Commitment Matlab code
Main script
In the main script of the power plant unit commitment, is the generally optimisation performed. First
is the data collected by the data import scripts imported. Thereafter is the paths for the optimisation
tools added. Next are all used parameters and other values stated, before the optimisation for loops
is ran. These for loops are ran while the total battery degradation DegTot is below 0.2.
When the battery degradation is greater than 0.2, the diﬀerent values are saved.
Main script for Power Plant Unit Commitment
1
close all; clear all; clc;
2
3
% Data load

load('LoadGenerationDataPP')
5
load('LoadDemandData')

% Modifying path to include the YALMIP toolbox

Fuel Use Calculation Matlab function
This Matlab function is used to calculate the actual fuel use of each of the ﬁve diesel engines. The
input in this function is the decision variable stating the active power production of each unit. Based
on this, the power production eﬃciency is calculated or decided ﬁrst, where after the real fuel use is
calculated for each of the ﬁve diesel engines. The output logged in the main script is then the total
daily real fuel use.
Fuel use calculation Matlab function
1

Battery Degradation Calculation Matlab function
This Matlab function calculates the battery degradation based on the inputs SOC and DegTot. First
the daily SOC curve is reduces to only contain the local maxima and minima points. This reduced
SOC curve is then inserted in the Matlab rainﬂow function. Based on the outputs from the rainﬂow
function, the DOD of each cycle and the cycle number, the battery degradation is calculated.
the battery cyclic degradation is added the calculated calender degradation at the end of the function.
The cyclic degradation is at the end multiplied with 0.2 because a 100% degradation is seen as a 20%
decrease in battery energy capacity. The calculated degradation in the speciﬁc day is the output of
the Degradation function and this output is added to the cumulated degradation in the main script.
Battery degradation calculation Matlab function
1
function Deg = Degradation(SOC,DegTot)
2
3
SOC = double(SOC);
4
5
%% Cyclic degradation
6
% Rainbow count
7
LocalMax
= findpeaks(SOC);
8
LocalMin
= findpeaks(SOC*(-1))*(-1);
9
10
intSOC
= zeros(1,2+length(LocalMax)+length(LocalMin));
11
12
intSOC(1)
= SOC(1);
13
intSOC(end) = SOC(end);

Chapter A. Appendix

i = 1;

end
20

for n = 1:length(LocalMin)
22
intSOC(i+2) = LocalMin(n);
23
i = i+2;
24

27
DOD = 2.*Statistic(1,:);

for n=1:length(DODUnique)
31
Cyc(n) = sum(Statistic(3,DOD==DODUnique(n)));

39

40
a(1) = f(1)/0.1;
41
for n=2:10
42
a(n)=(f(n)-f(n-1))/0.1;
43

44

for m=1:10
46

for n=1:10

A.3.2
Power Grid Unit Commitment Matlab code
Main script
The main script for the power grid unit commitment model contains of two diﬀerent parts. After the
data has been imported, the unit commitment problem with DC power ﬂow constraints are solved.
The binary decision variables decided in this optimisation is then copied as constants to the unit
commitment problem with AC power ﬂow constraints, where after this is performed.
Main script for Power Grid Unit Commitment

A.8
PowerFactory Model
This appendix contains more detailed information regarding the various PowerFactory models used
in this project.
A.8.1
BESS model
Where the BESS composite frames has been explained in chapter 5, this section will focus on describ-
ing the common models as in the template and thus also focusing on the original BESS composite
frame as shown in ﬁgure A.1
==PAGE:169==
Chapter A. Appendix
151
Figure A.1: BESS composite frame as from template
Common models
A common model is an element based on a block deﬁnition. It can contain two diﬀerent methods
of coding. 1 By the use of "Block References" or 2 A primitive way of deﬁning a set of equations
along with a set of variables (output signals, input signals, state variables, parameters and internal
variables).
The common models in the BESS templates mainly use "Block References" which is more illustrative.
The primitive way is also used but only to ensure initial criteria, it will therefore not be further shown
or elaborated.
Battery model - ElmDsl
To illustrate the behaviour of an battery the battery model is used. The idea behind this common
model is to determine the battery model output (UDC, Ucell SOC ,Icell) from the input IDC which is
the DC current that the battery is delivering to the inverter. This approach is valid assuming that
the BMS manage to load the cells evenly.
==PAGE:170==
Chapter A. Appendix
152
Figure A.2: Block references of battery model - ElmDsl
Along with the block references as indicated on ﬁgure A.2 the battery model requires some parameters
from the the battery manufacture The parameters needed by the battery model is shown in ﬁgure
A.3.
Figure A.3: Parameter list of battery model - ElmDsl
If the type of battery changes, the parameters listed on ﬁgure A.3 has to be updated.
Icell
As mentioned earlier then the cell current Icell is found from the total amount of cells in parallel and
the total IDC from the battery, this is shown in equation A.5.The cell current is then used to deﬁne
the SOC by the use of an integrator and an inverse gain.
ICell =
IDC
CellsParallel
(A.5)
==PAGE:171==
Chapter A. Appendix
153
SOC
The current Icell is divided by −3.6 to convert it from [kA] to [Ah]. The sign is bound to the IDC
which was found from the active power output, in that way that the active power would be negative if
the battery is charging. This division is part of an dynamic system integrator (Laplace transformed)
which contains an initial value y0 that is connected with the initial SOC such that y0 =
SOC0
Cellcap from
ﬁgure A.3. The integrator in real time will therefore output each signal as a function of its initial
state: y(t) =
tR
t0
u(t)dt + y0
This integrator will therefore ensure that the code has a memory function and will decrease/increase
from its previous value. The ﬁnal step in the calculation of the SOC is to divide with the cell capacity
to obtain the SOC in [%] as seen in equation A.6
SOC = Icell
1
−3.6 · s ·
1
Cellcap
(A.6)
UCell
To calculate the cell voltage Ucell the cell current is used to calculate the voltage drop over the IR,
and the SOC is used such that it deﬁnes the percentage voltage between Umin and Umax. Lastly there
is a limiter to ensure against users who have inserted a too high IR which could result in a negative
cell voltage, which then would be limited to 0.
Ucell =

SOC · Umax + Umin(1 −SOC)

−Icell · IR
1000
(A.7)
Vterm
The last variable is the terminal voltage Vterm which is simply calculated by multiplying the amount
of cells in series CellsSeries with the cell voltage.
Vterm = CellsSeries · Ucell
(A.8)
Frequency Control - ElmDsl
The frequency control common model is based on the droop controller principle. Compared to the
battery model then the frequency controller only contain two parameters:
• A frequency deadband Dband measured in per units [pu]
• And a droop factor R in percentage [%]
Droop characteristic deﬁnes a linear relation between the system frequency and the power o the
BESS. The basic principle is that if a the frequency is low then the droop characteristic will ensure
the BESS to increase it active power production to counteract the low frequency. Opposite if the
frequency is high the power output would be lowered or even go into charging. How sensitive this
control is controlled through the R parameter which deﬁnes the percentage frequency deviation, that
==PAGE:172==
Chapter A. Appendix
154
would result in a response from no load to full load.
Figure A.4 shows the block references of the Frequency control common model, where it can be seen
that the inputs are f0 and frq with an output of ∆Pref
Figure A.4: Block references of Frequency Control - ElmDsl
Figure A.4 contains 3 diﬀerent block references where the droop block is the block that contains the
dynamic control. This is because the deadband block reference simply ensure that the frequency
control does not activate during frequency deviations higher/lower then the Dband parameter. If
this block wasn’t there the controller would react at the slightest deviation and causing stress on the
inverter and oscillations.
The oﬀset P0 is there to ensure the output ∆Pref is compensated after a load ﬂow calculation. This
is needed because the output of the droop is always zero after initialization, in that case the ﬁrst scan
deﬁnes P0 = ∆Pref which gives PowerFactory one additional iteration to update ∆Pref. ([27])
The output of the frequency controller is thus calculated by:
∆Pref = (f0 −frq)
R
(A.9)
Where ∆Pref is limited to -1 and 1 to ensure no set point beyond the active power limitations. As
an example then a frequency deviation of 1 Hz with a droop rating of 0.04 (2 HZ deviation):
∆Pref = (1 −0.98)
0.04
= 0.5
Results in an increase of 50 % active power delivered to the grid.
Lastly it should be noted that f0 does not appear as an input on ﬁgure 5.2, but it does appear as an
input on ﬁgure A.4. This input is set in initialization where it is set as frq at ﬁrst iteration. This
can be justiﬁed because of an load ﬂow analysis would ensure stable frequency before any dynamic
simulations is run.
PQ - Control - ElmDsl
The PQ - Control common model is implemented such that it contains 2 controller circuits, one
for active power control (idref) and one for reactive power control (iqref). These 2 diﬀerent block
reference paths are indicated on ﬁgure A.5.
Both power controls start with an input of a measurement value, Pin for the active power and vin
for the reactive power. Then they are compared with their respective reference values, ∆Pref for
active power and Vref for reactive power to ﬁnd the deviations. Here it should be noted that Vref is
for initialization purpose being set equal to Vin in ﬁrst iteration, just like in the P0 in the frequency
controller.
==PAGE:173==
Chapter A. Appendix
155
Figure A.5: Block references for PQ - Control ElmDsl
After the deviations is found they are in both controls ﬁltered through a ﬁrst order ﬁlter

1
1+sT

with time constants Tr and Trq parameter input of the common model as seen on ﬁgure A.6. When
choosing the values of Tr and Trq it should be known that the higher these time constant becomes,
the further the output signal will be moving away from the asymptote that is the input signal.
Next part of the control paths is the introduction of a PI- Controller1 Through these controllers
the PQ-control common model obtains control functionality that not only gives a set point, but
regulate with the measured process values taken into consideration. The idref control path includes
an error signal ∆i from the output of the charge control. This error signal is calculated in the Charge
controller, and it represents the diﬀerence between idref and the ﬁnale d axis current signal sent to
the converter.
The PI-controller uses Kp, Kq, Tip, Tiq, idmax, iqmax, iqmin, idmin from ﬁgure A.6. Where kp, kq is the
proportional gains, and Tip, Tiq is the integrator time constants. By increasing the proportional gains
the regulator rise time becomes faster, care should however be taken since a too high gain would
result in low damping and possibly an unstable regulator. The integrator ensures no oﬀset in the
regulation output, where the parameter integrator time constants deﬁnes how fast the controller reacts
to changes, care should be taken since if this parameter becomes too low could result in oscillations.
idmax, iqmax, iqmin, idmin gives the user the possibility to deﬁne the upper and lower limits.
In the iqref control path the PI controller is split up instead of using the inbuilt PI controller. This is
to introduce the ACdeadband parameter in the kq part, which gives the possibility to chose how active
the controller should be in voltage control.
1PI Controller theory [p.204 [46]]
==PAGE:174==
Chapter A. Appendix
156
Figure A.6: Parameter list of PQ Control - ElmDsl
Figure A.6 shows the parameter list of the PQ-Control common model along with the predeﬁned
parameter values. It is here the tuning of the P and Q controllers can be performed, by tuning the
diﬀerent parameters.

A.8.4
PhotovoltaicSystem_0.4kV_0.5MVA
The PV model has gone through various modiﬁcations before being used in the capacity ﬁrming
simulations.
The assumptions behind these modiﬁcations was that it is not wanted to have an
intelligent PV control system, it is simply supposed to output what produced and nothing more.
This results in the modiﬁed PV composite frame as seen on ﬁgure A.15
Figure A.15: Modiﬁed "PhotovoltaicSystem_0.4kV_0.5MVA" composite frame
Changes done to the frame are as follows:
1. Replacing the solar radiation and temperature ramps with measurements ﬁle.
2. Simplifying the DC busbar by assuming steady state voltage
3. Removing active power measurements since the idref control loop in the controller were being
modiﬁed and measurements were not needed.
4. Modifying the idref control loop to control after Iarray·Vmpp_array
Pn
==PAGE:182==
Chapter A. Appendix
164
The controller on ﬁgure A.15 is working with the same principle as the BESS where the control is
done by idref and iqref and since the point of interest is active power it is only the idref loop that
has been changed, where all voltage control has been removed by alternating parameters.
The used controller can be seen on ﬁgure A.16:
Figure A.16: Modiﬁed Controller
As can be seen from ﬁgure A.16 then the output of the PV model are deﬁned by the two signals
Iarray Vmpp_array. These signals are calculated inside the photovoltaic model which uses a single diode
equivalent circuit. Following equations are used in the photovoltaic model:
1. Open circuit voltage
Calculating the open circuit voltage, which corresponds to the voltage drop over the diode and thus
reﬂects the voltage of the cell:
Uoc = Ul0_stc ·

condition open circuit voltage , which is at rated irradiance level of E = 1000 W
m2, multiplied with a
temperature correction factor, that introduces the eﬃciency change as a result of temperature input.

The short circuit current represent the highest current that a cell can produce. It is calculated from
